</td></tr>

<td colspan="2" style="text-align: center"><span class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Frameless"><a href="/wiki/File:Bat_range.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Bat_range.png/250px-Bat_range.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="115" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Bat_range.png/330px-Bat_range.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Bat_range.png/500px-Bat_range.png 2x" data-file-width="1425" data-file-height="745" /></a></span>

<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align: center; font-size: 88%">Worldwide distribution of bat species
</td></tr>












</tbody></table><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1238732961">@media screen{html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota tr{background:transparent!important}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota img{background:transparent}}@media screen and (prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota tr{background:transparent!important}html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota img{background:white}}.mw-parser-output .infobox.biota .taxobox-edit-taxonomy img{background:transparent!important}body.skin-vector .mw-parser-output table.biota.infobox{margin-top:0.5em}body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output table.biota.infobox tr.taxonrow td{padding:2px 10px}</style>
<p><b>Bats</b> are flying <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">order</a> <b>Chiroptera</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="/aɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;tide&#39;">aɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;r&#39; in &#39;rye&#39;">r</span><span title="/ɒ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;body&#39;">ɒ</span><span title="&#39;p&#39; in &#39;pie&#39;">p</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/ər/: &#39;er&#39; in &#39;letter&#39;">ər</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span>).<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>a<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> With their forelimbs adapted as <a href="/wiki/Bat_wing_development" title="Bat wing development">wings</a>, they are the only mammals capable of true and sustained <a href="/wiki/Bat_flight" title="Bat flight">flight</a>. Bats are more agile in flight than most birds, flying with their very long spread-out digits covered with a thin membrane or <a href="/wiki/Patagium" title="Patagium">patagium</a>. The smallest bat, and arguably the <a href="/wiki/Smallest_organisms" title="Smallest organisms">smallest extant mammal</a>, is <a href="/wiki/Kitti%27s_hog-nosed_bat" title="Kitti&#39;s hog-nosed bat">Kitti's hog-nosed bat</a>, which is 29–34&#160;mm (1.1–1.3&#160;in) in length, 150&#160;mm (5.9&#160;in) across the wings and 2–2.6&#160;g (0.071–0.092&#160;oz) in mass. The largest bats are the <a href="/wiki/Flying_foxes" class="mw-redirect" title="Flying foxes">flying foxes</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/Giant_golden-crowned_flying_fox" title="Giant golden-crowned flying fox">giant golden-crowned flying fox</a> (<i>Acerodon jubatus</i>) reaching a weight of 1.6&#160;kg (3.5&#160;lb) and having a wingspan of 1.7&#160;m (5&#160;ft 7&#160;in).
</p><p>The second largest order of mammals after <a href="/wiki/Rodents" class="mw-redirect" title="Rodents">rodents</a>, bats comprise about 20% of all classified mammal species worldwide, with over 1,400 species. These were traditionally divided into two suborders: the largely fruit-eating <a href="/wiki/Megabat" title="Megabat">megabats</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Animal_echolocation" title="Animal echolocation">echolocating</a> <a href="/wiki/Microbat" title="Microbat">microbats</a>. But more recent evidence has supported dividing the order into <a href="/wiki/Yinpterochiroptera" title="Yinpterochiroptera">Yinpterochiroptera</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yangochiroptera" title="Yangochiroptera">Yangochiroptera</a>, with megabats as members of the former along with several species of microbats. Many bats are <a href="/wiki/Insectivore" title="Insectivore">insectivores</a>, and most of the rest are <a href="/wiki/Frugivore" title="Frugivore">frugivores</a> (fruit-eaters) or <a href="/wiki/Nectarivore" title="Nectarivore">nectarivores</a> (nectar-eaters). A few species feed on animals other than insects; for example, the <a href="/wiki/Vampire_bat" title="Vampire bat">vampire bats</a> <a href="/wiki/Hematophagy" title="Hematophagy">feed on blood</a>. Most bats are <a href="/wiki/Nocturnal" class="mw-redirect" title="Nocturnal">nocturnal</a>, and many roost in caves or other refuges; it is uncertain whether bats have these behaviours to escape <a href="/wiki/Predator" class="mw-redirect" title="Predator">predators</a>. Bats are present throughout the world, with the exception of extremely cold regions. They are important in their ecosystems for <a href="/wiki/Pollinator" title="Pollinator">pollinating</a> <a href="/wiki/Flower" title="Flower">flowers</a> and dispersing seeds; many tropical plants depend entirely on bats for these services.
</p><p>Bats provide humans with some direct benefits, at the cost of some disadvantages. Bat dung has been mined as <a href="/wiki/Guano" title="Guano">guano</a> from caves and used as fertiliser. Bats consume insect pests, reducing the need for <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a> and other insect management measures. Some bats are also predators of <a href="/wiki/Mosquito" title="Mosquito">mosquitoes</a>, suppressing the transmission of <a href="/wiki/Mosquito-borne_disease" title="Mosquito-borne disease">mosquito-borne diseases</a>. Bats are sometimes numerous enough and close enough to human settlements to serve as tourist attractions, and they are used as food across Asia and the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Rim" title="Pacific Rim">Pacific Rim</a>. However, fruit bats are frequently considered pests by fruit growers. Due to their physiology, bats are one type of animal that acts as a <a href="/wiki/Natural_reservoir" title="Natural reservoir">natural reservoir</a> of many <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogens</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Rabies" title="Rabies">rabies</a>; and since they are highly mobile, social, and long-lived, they can readily spread disease among themselves. If humans interact with bats, these traits become potentially dangerous to humans.
</p><p>Depending on the culture, bats may be symbolically associated with positive traits, such as protection from certain diseases or risks, rebirth, or long life, but in the West, bats are popularly associated with darkness, malevolence, witchcraft, <a href="/wiki/Vampire" title="Vampire">vampires</a>, and death.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Etymology">Etymology</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>An older English name for bats is <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/flittermouse" class="extiw" title="wikt:flittermouse">flittermouse</a>, which matches their name in other <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic languages</a> (for example German <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Fledermaus</i></span> and Swedish <span title="Swedish-language text"><i lang="sv">fladdermus</i></span>), related to the fluttering of wings. <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> had <span title="Middle English (1100-1500)-language text"><i lang="enm">bakke</i></span>, most likely cognate with Old Swedish <span title="Old Norse-language text"><i lang="non">natbakka</i></span> (<span class="gloss-quot">'</span><span class="gloss-text">night-bat</span><span class="gloss-quot">'</span>), which may have undergone a shift from <i>-k-</i> to <i>-t-</i> (to Modern English <i>bat</i>) influenced by Latin <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">blatta</i></span>, <span class="gloss-quot">'</span><span class="gloss-text">moth, nocturnal insect</span><span class="gloss-quot">'</span>. The word <i>bat</i> was probably first used in the early 1570s.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The name <i>Chiroptera</i> derives from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">χείρ</span> – <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><i lang="grc-Latn">kheír</i></span>, <span class="gloss-quot">'</span><span class="gloss-text">hand</span><span class="gloss-quot">'</span><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">πτερόν</span></span> – <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><i lang="grc-Latn">pterón</i></span>, <span class="gloss-quot">'</span><span class="gloss-text">wing</span><span class="gloss-quot">'</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-EB1911_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Phylogeny_and_taxonomy">Phylogeny and taxonomy</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Phylogeny and taxonomy"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Batfossil.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Batfossil.jpg/250px-Batfossil.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="240" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Batfossil.jpg/330px-Batfossil.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Batfossil.jpg/340px-Batfossil.jpg 2x" data-file-width="401" data-file-height="567" /></a><figcaption>The early <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a> fossil microchiropteran <i><a href="/wiki/Icaronycteris" title="Icaronycteris">Icaronycteris</a></i>, from the <a href="/wiki/Green_River_Formation" title="Green River Formation">Green River Formation</a></figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Evolution">Evolution</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Evolution"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The delicate <a href="/wiki/Skeleton" title="Skeleton">skeletons</a> of bats do not fossilise well; it is estimated that only 12% of bat <a href="/wiki/Genera" class="mw-redirect" title="Genera">genera</a> that lived have been found in the fossil record.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Most of the oldest known bat fossils were already very similar to modern microbats, such as <i>Archaeopteropus</i> (32 million years ago). The oldest known bat fossils include <i>Archaeonycteris praecursor</i> and <i>Altaynycteris aurora</i> (55–56 million years ago), both known only from isolated teeth.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The oldest complete bat skeleton is <i><a href="/wiki/Icaronycteris" title="Icaronycteris">Icaronycteris</a> gunnelli</i> (52 million years ago), known from two skeletons discovered in Wyoming.<sup id="cite_ref-simmons2008_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-simmons2008-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The extinct bats <i><a href="/wiki/Palaeochiropteryx_tupaiodon" class="mw-redirect" title="Palaeochiropteryx tupaiodon">Palaeochiropteryx tupaiodon</a></i>  and <i><a href="/wiki/Hassianycteris_kumari" class="mw-redirect" title="Hassianycteris kumari">Hassianycteris kumari</a></i>, both of which lived 48&#160;million years ago, are the first fossil mammals whose colouration has been discovered: both were reddish-brown.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Bats were formerly grouped in the superorder <a href="/wiki/Archonta" title="Archonta">Archonta</a>, along with the <a href="/wiki/Treeshrew" title="Treeshrew">treeshrews</a> (Scandentia), <a href="/wiki/Colugo" title="Colugo">colugos</a> (Dermoptera), and <a href="/wiki/Primate" title="Primate">primates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Modern genetic evidence now places bats in the superorder <a href="/wiki/Laurasiatheria" title="Laurasiatheria">Laurasiatheria</a>, with its <a href="/wiki/Sister_taxon" class="mw-redirect" title="Sister taxon">sister taxon</a> as <a href="/wiki/Ferungulata" title="Ferungulata">Ferungulata</a>, which includes <a href="/wiki/Carnivora" title="Carnivora">carnivorans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pangolin" title="Pangolin">pangolins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Odd-toed_ungulate" class="mw-redirect" title="Odd-toed ungulate">odd-toed ungulates</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Even-toed_ungulate" class="mw-redirect" title="Even-toed ungulate">even-toed ungulates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bat_Schematics_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bat_Schematics-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-classification_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-classification-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pumo1998_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pumo1998-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zhou2011_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zhou2011-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tsagkogeorga_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tsagkogeorga-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> One study places Chiroptera as a sister taxon to odd-toed ungulates (Perissodactyla).<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<tr>
<td style="text-align: left; font-size: 88%; border: 0; padding: 0;"><a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_tree" title="Phylogenetic tree">Phylogenetic tree</a> showing Chiroptera within <a href="/wiki/Laurasiatheria" title="Laurasiatheria">Laurasiatheria</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Fereuungulata" class="mw-redirect" title="Fereuungulata">Fereuungulata</a> as its <a href="/wiki/Sister_taxon" class="mw-redirect" title="Sister taxon">sister taxon</a> according to a 2013 study<sup id="cite_ref-Tsagkogeorga_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tsagkogeorga-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div style="clear:both;" class=""></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Flying_primate_hypothesis" title="Flying primate hypothesis">flying primate hypothesis</a> proposed that when adaptations to flight are removed, megabats are allied to <a href="/wiki/Primate" title="Primate">primates</a> by anatomical features not shared with microbats and thus flight evolved twice in mammals.<sup id="cite_ref-Bailey1992_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey1992-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Genetic studies have strongly supported the <a href="/wiki/Monophyly" title="Monophyly">monophyly</a> of bats and the single origin of mammal flight.<sup id="cite_ref-simmons2008_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-simmons2008-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bailey1992_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey1992-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Coevolutionary_evidence">Coevolutionary evidence</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Coevolutionary evidence"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>An independent molecular analysis trying to establish the dates when bat ectoparasites (<a href="/wiki/Cimicidae" title="Cimicidae">bedbugs</a>) evolved came to the conclusion that bedbugs similar to those known today (all major extant lineages, all of which feed primarily on bats) had already diversified and become established over 100 mya (i.e., long before the oldest records for bats, 52 mya), suggesting that they initially all evolved on non-bat hosts and "bats were colonized several times independently, unless the evolutionary origin of bats has been grossly underestimated."<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> No analysis has provided estimates for the age of the flea lineages associated with bats. The oldest known members of a different lineage of bat ectoparasites (<a href="/wiki/Streblidae" title="Streblidae">bat flies</a>), however, are from roughly 20 mya, well after the origin of bats.<sup id="cite_ref-Poinar&amp;Brown2012_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poinar&amp;Brown2012-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The bat-ectoparasitic <a href="/wiki/Earwig" title="Earwig">earwig</a> family <a href="/wiki/Arixeniidae" title="Arixeniidae">Arixeniidae</a> has no fossil record, but is not believed to originate more than 23 mya.<sup id="cite_ref-EvolutionoftheInsects_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EvolutionoftheInsects-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Inner_systematics">Inner systematics</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Inner systematics"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>

<tr>
<td style="text-align: left; font-size: 88%; border: 0; padding: 0;">Internal relationships of the Chiroptera, divided into the traditional megabat and microbat clades, according to a 2011 study<sup id="cite_ref-agnarsson_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-agnarsson-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div style="clear:both;" class=""></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">Genetic</a> evidence indicates that megabats originated during the early <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a>, and belong within the four major lines of microbats.<sup id="cite_ref-Tsagkogeorga_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tsagkogeorga-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Two new suborders have been proposed; <a href="/wiki/Yinpterochiroptera" title="Yinpterochiroptera">Yinpterochiroptera</a> includes the <a href="/wiki/Megabat" title="Megabat">Pteropodidae</a>, or megabat family, as well as the families <a href="/wiki/Horseshoe_bat" title="Horseshoe bat">Rhinolophidae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hipposideridae" title="Hipposideridae">Hipposideridae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Craseonycteridae" class="mw-redirect" title="Craseonycteridae">Craseonycteridae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Megadermatidae" title="Megadermatidae">Megadermatidae</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rhinopomatidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhinopomatidae">Rhinopomatidae</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Teeling-etal-2005_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Teeling-etal-2005-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Yangochiroptera" title="Yangochiroptera">Yangochiroptera</a> includes the other families of bats (all of which use laryngeal echolocation), a conclusion supported by a 2005 DNA study.<sup id="cite_ref-Teeling-etal-2005_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Teeling-etal-2005-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A 2013 phylogenomic study supported the two new proposed suborders.<sup id="cite_ref-Tsagkogeorga_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tsagkogeorga-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<table style="border: 1px solid #ccc; vertical-align: middle; width: 248px; margin:0.2em 1em 0.2em 0; float:left; clear:left; width:700px;font-size:85%;line-height:75%">



<tbody><tr>
<td style="padding: 5px">

</td>
<td rowspan="2" class="clade-leaf">
<p><a href="/wiki/Megadermatidae" title="Megadermatidae">Megadermatidae</a> (false vampire bats) <span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Megaderma_spasma.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Megaderma_spasma.jpg/60px-Megaderma_spasma.jpg" decoding="async" width="60" height="61" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Megaderma_spasma.jpg/120px-Megaderma_spasma.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1402" data-file-height="1424" /></a></span>
</p>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-slabel">
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-label">
</td>
<td rowspan="2" class="clade-leaf">
<p>horseshoe bats and allies <span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Rhinolophus_rouxii.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Rhinolophus_rouxii.jpg/40px-Rhinolophus_rouxii.jpg" decoding="async" width="40" height="60" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Rhinolophus_rouxii.jpg/60px-Rhinolophus_rouxii.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Rhinolophus_rouxii.jpg/120px-Rhinolophus_rouxii.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1206" data-file-height="1796" /></a></span>
</p>
</td></tr>

</td></tr>


<tr>
<td style="text-align: left; font-size: 88%; border: 0; padding: 0;">Internal relationships of the Chiroptera, with the megabats subsumed within Yinpterochiroptera, according to a 2013 study<sup id="cite_ref-Tsagkogeorga_19-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tsagkogeorga-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</td></tr></tbody></table><div style="clear:both;" class=""></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Golden_crowned_fruit_bat.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Golden_crowned_fruit_bat.jpg/130px-Golden_crowned_fruit_bat.jpg" decoding="async" width="130" height="207" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Golden_crowned_fruit_bat.jpg/195px-Golden_crowned_fruit_bat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3f/Golden_crowned_fruit_bat.jpg 2x" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="319" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Giant_golden-crowned_flying_fox" title="Giant golden-crowned flying fox">Giant golden-crowned flying fox</a>, <i>Acerodon jubatus</i></figcaption></figure>
<p>The 2003 discovery of an early fossil bat from the 52-million-year-old <a href="/wiki/Green_River_Formation" title="Green River Formation">Green River Formation</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Onychonycteris" title="Onychonycteris">Onychonycteris finneyi</a></i>, indicates that flight evolved before echolocative abilities.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Onychonycteris</i> had claws on all five of its fingers, whereas modern bats have at most two claws on two digits of each hand. It also had longer hind legs and shorter forearms, similar to climbing mammals that hang under branches, such as <a href="/wiki/Sloth" title="Sloth">sloths</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gibbon" title="Gibbon">gibbons</a>. This palm-sized bat had short, broad wings, suggesting that it could not fly as fast or as far as later bat species. Instead of flapping its wings continuously while flying, <i>Onychonycteris</i> probably alternated between flaps and glides in the air.<sup id="cite_ref-simmons2008_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-simmons2008-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This suggests that this bat did not fly as much as modern bats, but flew from tree to tree and spent most of its time climbing or hanging on branches.<sup id="cite_ref-norberg_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-norberg-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The distinctive features of the <i>Onychonycteris</i> fossil also support the hypothesis that mammalian flight most likely evolved in arboreal locomotors, rather than terrestrial runners. This model of flight development, commonly known as the "trees-down" theory, holds that bats first flew by taking advantage of height and gravity to drop down on to prey, rather than running fast enough for a ground-level take off.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kaplan2011_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kaplan2011-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<table style="border: 1px solid #ccc; vertical-align: middle; width: 248px; margin:0.2em 1em 0.2em 0; float:left; clear:left; width:700px;font-size:85%;line-height:75%">

<tr>
<td style="text-align: left; font-size: 88%; border: 0; padding: 0;">Familial relationships of bats according to a 2023 study using mitochondrial and nuclear DNA from 151 species. Two different analysis methods were used which resulted in almost identical trees, except for the position of Emballonuroidea and the relationships between Rhinolophidae, Hipposideridae, and Rhinonycteridae, which are represented as <a href="/wiki/Polytomy" title="Polytomy">polytomies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</td></tr></tbody></table><div style="clear:both;" class=""></div>
<p>The molecular phylogeny was controversial, as it pointed to microbats <a href="/wiki/Paraphyly" title="Paraphyly">not having a unique common ancestry</a>, which implied that some seemingly unlikely transformations occurred. The first is that laryngeal echolocation evolved twice in bats, once in Yangochiroptera and once in the rhinolophoids.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The second is that laryngeal echolocation had a single origin in Chiroptera, was subsequently lost in the family Pteropodidae (all megabats), and later evolved as a system of tongue-clicking in the genus <i>Rousettus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Analyses of the sequence of the vocalization gene <i>FoxP2</i> were inconclusive on whether laryngeal echolocation was lost in the pteropodids or gained in the echolocating lineages.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Echolocation probably first derived in bats from communicative calls. The Eocene bats <i><a href="/wiki/Icaronycteris" title="Icaronycteris">Icaronycteris</a></i> (52&#160;million years ago) and <i><a href="/wiki/Palaeochiropteryx" title="Palaeochiropteryx">Palaeochiropteryx</a></i> had cranial adaptations suggesting an ability to detect <a href="/wiki/Ultrasound" title="Ultrasound">ultrasound</a>. This may have been used at first mainly to forage on the ground for insects and map out their surroundings in their gliding phase, or for communicative purposes. After the adaptation of flight was established, it may have been refined to target flying prey by echolocation.<sup id="cite_ref-norberg_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-norberg-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Analyses of the hearing gene <i>Prestin</i> seem to favour the idea that echolocation developed independently at least twice, rather than being lost secondarily in the pteropodids,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but <a href="/wiki/Ontogeny" title="Ontogeny">ontogenic</a> analysis of the cochlea supports that laryngeal echolocation evolved only once.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Classification">Classification</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Classification"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_chiropterans" title="List of chiropterans">List of chiropterans</a></div>
<p>Bats are <a href="/wiki/Placental_mammal" class="mw-redirect" title="Placental mammal">placental mammals</a>. After <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodents</a>, they are the largest <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">order</a>, making up about 20% of mammal species.<sup id="cite_ref-LeiDong2016_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LeiDong2016-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1758, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> classified the seven bat species he knew of in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Vespertilio" title="Vespertilio">Vespertilio</a></i> in the order <a href="/wiki/Primates" class="mw-redirect" title="Primates">Primates</a>. Around twenty years later, the German naturalist <a href="/wiki/Johann_Friedrich_Blumenbach" title="Johann Friedrich Blumenbach">Johann Friedrich Blumenbach</a> gave them their own order, Chiroptera.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Since then, the number of described species has risen to over 1,400,<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> traditionally classified as two suborders: <a href="/wiki/Megabat" title="Megabat">Megachiroptera</a> (megabats), and <a href="/wiki/Microbat" title="Microbat">Microchiroptera</a> (microbats/echolocating bats).<sup id="cite_ref-prothero_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prothero-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Not all megabats are larger than microbats.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Several characteristics distinguish the two groups. Microbats use <a href="/wiki/Animal_echolocation" title="Animal echolocation">echolocation</a> for navigation and finding prey, but megabats apart from those in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Rousettus" title="Rousettus">Rousettus</a></i> do not.<sup id="cite_ref-Holland_2004_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holland_2004-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Accordingly, megabats have a well-developed eyesight.<sup id="cite_ref-prothero_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prothero-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Megabats have a claw on the second finger of the forelimb.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Olney_1994_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Olney_1994-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The external ears of microbats do not close to form a ring; the edges are separated from each other at the base of the ear.<sup id="cite_ref-Olney_1994_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Olney_1994-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Megabats <a href="/wiki/Frugivory" class="mw-redirect" title="Frugivory">eat fruit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nectarivore" title="Nectarivore">nectar</a>, or pollen, while most microbats <a href="/wiki/Insectivory" class="mw-redirect" title="Insectivory">eat insects</a>; others feed on fruit, nectar, pollen, <a href="/wiki/Piscivore" title="Piscivore">fish</a>, frogs, small mammals, or <a href="/wiki/Hematophagy" title="Hematophagy">blood</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-prothero_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prothero-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Haeckel_Chiroptera.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Haeckel_Chiroptera.jpg/250px-Haeckel_Chiroptera.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="237" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Haeckel_Chiroptera.jpg/330px-Haeckel_Chiroptera.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Haeckel_Chiroptera.jpg/500px-Haeckel_Chiroptera.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2314" data-file-height="3220" /></a><figcaption>"Chiroptera" from <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Haeckel" title="Ernst Haeckel">Ernst Haeckel</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Kunstformen_der_Natur" title="Kunstformen der Natur">Kunstformen der Natur</a></i>, 1904</figcaption></figure>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Anatomy_and_physiology">Anatomy and physiology</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Anatomy and physiology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Skull_and_dentition">Skull and dentition</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Skull and dentition"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Horniman_fruit_bat_skin_skeleton.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Horniman_fruit_bat_skin_skeleton.jpg/250px-Horniman_fruit_bat_skin_skeleton.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="176" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Horniman_fruit_bat_skin_skeleton.jpg/330px-Horniman_fruit_bat_skin_skeleton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Horniman_fruit_bat_skin_skeleton.jpg/500px-Horniman_fruit_bat_skin_skeleton.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="2048" /></a><figcaption>A preserved megabat showing how the skeleton fits inside its skin</figcaption></figure>
<p>The head and teeth shape of bats can vary by species. In general, megabats have longer snouts, larger eye sockets and smaller ears, giving them a more dog-like appearance, which is the source of their nickname of "flying foxes".<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Among microbats, longer snouts are associated with nectar-feeding,<sup id="cite_ref-MacDonald_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> while vampire bats have reduced snouts to accommodate large incisors and canines.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenhall1983_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenhall1983-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Small insect-eating bats can have as many as 38 teeth, while vampire bats have only 20. Bats that feed on hard-shelled insects have fewer but larger teeth with longer canines and more robust lower jaws than species that prey on softer bodied insects. In nectar-feeding bats, the canines are long while the cheek-teeth are reduced. In fruit-eating bats, the cusps of the cheek teeth are adapted for crushing.<sup id="cite_ref-MacDonald_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The upper incisors of vampire bats lack <a href="/wiki/Tooth_enamel" title="Tooth enamel">enamel</a>, which keeps them razor-sharp.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenhall1983_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenhall1983-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The bite force of small bats is generated through <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_advantage" title="Mechanical advantage">mechanical advantage</a>, allowing them to bite through the <a href="/wiki/Sclerotization" title="Sclerotization">hardened</a> armour of insects or the <a href="/wiki/Peel_(fruit)" title="Peel (fruit)">skin</a> of fruit.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Wings_and_flight">Wings and flight</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Wings and flight"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Bat_flight" title="Bat flight">Bat flight</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bat_wing_development" title="Bat wing development">Bat wing development</a></div>
<p>Bats are the only mammals capable of sustained flight, as opposed to <a href="/wiki/Glide_(flight)" class="mw-redirect" title="Glide (flight)">gliding</a>, as in the <a href="/wiki/Flying_squirrel" title="Flying squirrel">flying squirrel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-molecule_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-molecule-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The fastest bat, the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_free-tailed_bat" title="Mexican free-tailed bat">Mexican free-tailed bat</a> (<i>Tadarida brasiliensis</i>), can achieve a <a href="/wiki/Ground_speed" title="Ground speed">ground speed</a> of 160&#160;km/h (100&#160;mph).<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><span><video id="mwe_player_0" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Flapping-Tail-Membrane-in-Bats-Produces-Potentially-Important-Thrust-during-Horizontal-Takeoffs-and-pone.0032074.s004.ogv/220px--Flapping-Tail-Membrane-in-Bats-Produces-Potentially-Important-Thrust-during-Horizontal-Takeoffs-and-pone.0032074.s004.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" data-mw-tmh="" class="mw-file-element" width="220" height="150" data-durationhint="9" data-mwtitle="Flapping-Tail-Membrane-in-Bats-Produces-Potentially-Important-Thrust-during-Horizontal-Takeoffs-and-pone.0032074.s004.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" resource="/wiki/File:Flapping-Tail-Membrane-in-Bats-Produces-Potentially-Important-Thrust-during-Horizontal-Takeoffs-and-pone.0032074.s004.ogv"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/5/5c/Flapping-Tail-Membrane-in-Bats-Produces-Potentially-Important-Thrust-during-Horizontal-Takeoffs-and-pone.0032074.s004.ogv/Flapping-Tail-Membrane-in-Bats-Produces-Potentially-Important-Thrust-during-Horizontal-Takeoffs-and-pone.0032074.s004.ogv.240p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="240p.vp9.webm" data-width="352" data-height="240" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/5/5c/Flapping-Tail-Membrane-in-Bats-Produces-Potentially-Important-Thrust-during-Horizontal-Takeoffs-and-pone.0032074.s004.ogv/Flapping-Tail-Membrane-in-Bats-Produces-Potentially-Important-Thrust-during-Horizontal-Takeoffs-and-pone.0032074.s004.ogv.360p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp8, vorbis&quot;" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="352" data-height="240" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5c/Flapping-Tail-Membrane-in-Bats-Produces-Potentially-Important-Thrust-during-Horizontal-Takeoffs-and-pone.0032074.s004.ogv" type="video/ogg; codecs=&quot;theora&quot;" data-width="352" data-height="240" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/5/5c/Flapping-Tail-Membrane-in-Bats-Produces-Potentially-Important-Thrust-during-Horizontal-Takeoffs-and-pone.0032074.s004.ogv/Flapping-Tail-Membrane-in-Bats-Produces-Potentially-Important-Thrust-during-Horizontal-Takeoffs-and-pone.0032074.s004.ogv.144p.mjpeg.mov" type="video/quicktime" data-transcodekey="144p.mjpeg.mov" data-width="212" data-height="144" /></video></span><figcaption>Little brown bat take-off and flight</figcaption></figure>
<p>The finger bones of bats are much more flexible than those of other mammals, owing to their flattened cross-section and to low levels of <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a> near their tips.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The elongation of bat digits, a key feature required for wing development, is due to the <a href="/wiki/Upregulation" class="mw-redirect" title="Upregulation">upregulation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bone_morphogenetic_protein" title="Bone morphogenetic protein">bone morphogenetic proteins</a> (Bmps). During <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryonic development</a>, the gene controlling Bmp signalling, <i><a href="/wiki/Bone_morphogenetic_protein_2" title="Bone morphogenetic protein 2">Bmp2</a></i>, is subjected to increased expression in bat forelimbs&#160;&#8211;&#32;resulting in the extension of the manual digits. This crucial genetic alteration helps create the specialized limbs required for powered flight. The relative proportion of extant bat forelimb digits compared with those of Eocene fossil bats have no significant differences, suggesting that bat wing morphology has been conserved for over fifty million years.<sup id="cite_ref-Sears2006_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sears2006-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> During flight, the bones undergo <a href="/wiki/Bending" title="Bending">bending</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shear_stress" title="Shear stress">shearing</a> <a href="/wiki/Stress_(mechanics)" title="Stress (mechanics)">stress</a>; the bending stresses felt are smaller than in terrestrial mammals, but the shearing stress is larger. The wing bones of bats have a slightly lower breaking stress point than those of birds.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>As in other mammals, and unlike in birds, the <a href="/wiki/Radius_(anatomy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Radius (anatomy)">radius</a> is the main component of the forearm. Bats have five elongated digits, which all radiate around the wrist. The thumb points forward and supports the <a href="/wiki/Leading_edge" title="Leading edge">leading edge</a> of the wing, and the other digits support the tension held in the wing membrane. The second and third digits go along the wing tip, allowing the wing to be pulled forward against aerodynamic <a href="/wiki/Drag_(physics)" title="Drag (physics)">drag</a>, without having to be thick as in <a href="/wiki/Pterosaur" title="Pterosaur">pterosaur</a> wings. The fourth and fifth digits go from the wrist to the <a href="/wiki/Trailing_edge" title="Trailing edge">trailing edge</a>, and repel the bending force caused by air pushing up against the stiff membrane.<sup id="cite_ref-pennycuick_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pennycuick-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Due to their flexible joints, bats are more maneuverable and more dexterous than gliding mammals.<sup id="cite_ref-marshall2015_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-marshall2015-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Big-eared-townsend-fledermaus.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Big-eared-townsend-fledermaus.jpg/250px-Big-eared-townsend-fledermaus.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="134" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Big-eared-townsend-fledermaus.jpg/330px-Big-eared-townsend-fledermaus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Big-eared-townsend-fledermaus.jpg/500px-Big-eared-townsend-fledermaus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="617" data-file-height="375" /></a><figcaption>Wing membranes (<a href="/wiki/Patagium" title="Patagium">patagia</a>) of <a href="/wiki/Townsend%27s_big-eared_bat" title="Townsend&#39;s big-eared bat">Townsend's big-eared bat</a>, <i>Corynorhinus townsendii</i></figcaption></figure>
<p>The wings of bats are much thinner and consist of more bones than the wings of birds, allowing bats to maneuver more accurately than the latter, and fly with more lift and less drag.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By folding the wings in toward their bodies on the upstroke, they save 35 percent energy during flight.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The membranes are delicate, tearing easily,<sup id="cite_ref-ideas_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ideas-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but can regrow, and small tears heal quickly.<sup id="cite_ref-ideas_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ideas-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The surface of the wings is equipped with touch-sensitive receptors on small bumps called <a href="/wiki/Merkel_cell" title="Merkel cell">Merkel cells</a>, also found on human fingertips. These sensitive areas are different in bats, as each bump has a tiny hair in the center, making it even more sensitive and allowing the bat to detect and adapt to changing airflow; the primary use is to judge the most efficient speed at which to fly, and possibly also to avoid <a href="/wiki/Stall_(fluid_mechanics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Stall (fluid mechanics)">stalls</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sterbing_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sterbing-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Insectivorous bats may also use tactile hairs to help perform complex maneuvers to capture prey in flight.<sup id="cite_ref-marshall2015_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-marshall2015-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Skin"></span>
The <a href="/wiki/Patagium" title="Patagium">patagium</a> is the wing membrane; it is stretched between the arm and finger bones, and down the side of the body to the hind limbs and tail. This skin membrane consists of <a href="/wiki/Connective_tissue" title="Connective tissue">connective tissue</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elastic_fibre" class="mw-redirect" title="Elastic fibre">elastic fibres</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nerve" title="Nerve">nerves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">blood vessels</a>. The muscles keep the membrane taut during flight.<sup id="cite_ref-Mehlhorn2013_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mehlhorn2013-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The extent to which the tail of a bat is attached to a patagium can vary by species, with some having completely free tails or even no tails.<sup id="cite_ref-MacDonald_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The skin on the body of the bat, which has one layer of <a href="/wiki/Epidermis" title="Epidermis">epidermis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dermis" title="Dermis">dermis</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Hair_follicle" title="Hair follicle">hair follicles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweat_gland" title="Sweat gland">sweat glands</a> and a fatty subcutaneous layer, is very different from the skin of the wing membrane. Depending on the bat species the presence of hair follicles and sweat glands will vary in the patagium.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This patagium is an extremely thin double layer of epidermis; these layers are separated by a <a href="/wiki/Connective_tissue" title="Connective tissue">connective tissue</a> center, rich with <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elastic_fiber" title="Elastic fiber">elastic fibers</a>. In some bat species sweat glands will be present in between this connective tissue.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Furthermore, if hair follicles are present this supports the bat in order to adjust sudden flight maneuvers.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For bat embryos, <a href="/wiki/Apoptosis" title="Apoptosis">apoptosis</a> (programmed cell death) affects only the hindlimbs, while the forelimbs retain webbing between the digits that forms into the wing membranes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015166–167_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015166–167-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Unlike birds, whose stiff wings deliver bending and torsional stress to the shoulders, bats have a flexible wing membrane that can resist only tension. To achieve flight, a bat exerts force inwards at the points where the membrane meets the skeleton, so that an opposing force balances it on the wing edges perpendicular to the wing surface. This adaptation does not permit bats to reduce their wingspans, unlike birds, which can partly fold their wings in flight, radically reducing the wing span and area for the upstroke and for gliding. Hence bats cannot travel over long distances as birds can.<sup id="cite_ref-pennycuick_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pennycuick-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Nectar- and pollen-eating bats can hover, in a similar way to <a href="/wiki/Hummingbird" title="Hummingbird">hummingbirds</a>. The sharp leading edges of the wings can create <a href="/wiki/Vortex" title="Vortex">vortices</a>, which provide <a href="/wiki/Vortex_lift" title="Vortex lift">lift</a>. The vortex may be stabilized by the animal changing its wing curvatures.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Roosting_and_gaits">Roosting and gaits</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Roosting and gaits"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Group_flying_dogs_hanging_in_tree_Sri_Lanka.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Group_flying_dogs_hanging_in_tree_Sri_Lanka.JPG/330px-Group_flying_dogs_hanging_in_tree_Sri_Lanka.JPG" decoding="async" width="290" height="193" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Group_flying_dogs_hanging_in_tree_Sri_Lanka.JPG/500px-Group_flying_dogs_hanging_in_tree_Sri_Lanka.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Group_flying_dogs_hanging_in_tree_Sri_Lanka.JPG/960px-Group_flying_dogs_hanging_in_tree_Sri_Lanka.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1067" /></a><figcaption>Group of megabats roosting</figcaption></figure>
<p>When not flying, bats hang upside down from their feet, a posture known as roosting.<sup id="cite_ref-fenton1984_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fenton1984-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The femurs are attached at the hips in a way that allows them to bend outward and upward in flight. The ankle joint can flex to allow the trailing edge of the wings to bend downwards. This does not permit many movements other than hanging or clambering up trees.<sup id="cite_ref-pennycuick_57-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pennycuick-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Most megabats roost with the head tucked towards the belly, whereas most microbats roost with the neck curled towards the back. This difference is reflected in the structure of the <a href="/wiki/Cervical_vertebra" class="mw-redirect" title="Cervical vertebra">cervical or neck vertebrae</a> in the two groups, which are clearly distinct.<sup id="cite_ref-fenton1984_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fenton1984-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Tendons allow bats to lock their feet closed when hanging from a roost. Muscular power is needed to let go, but not to grasp a perch or when holding on.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons201578_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons201578-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>When on the ground, most bats can only crawl awkwardly. A few species such as the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_lesser_short-tailed_bat" title="New Zealand lesser short-tailed bat">New Zealand lesser short-tailed bat</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Common_vampire_bat" title="Common vampire bat">common vampire bat</a> are agile on the ground. Both species make lateral gaits (the limbs move one after the other) when moving slowly but vampire bats move with a bounding gait (all limbs move in unison) at greater speeds, the folded up wings being used to propel them forward. Vampire bats likely evolved these gaits to follow their hosts while short-tailed bats developed in the absence of terrestrial mammal competitors. Enhanced terrestrial locomotion does not appear to have reduced their ability to fly.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Internal_systems">Internal systems</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Internal systems"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Bats have an efficient <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">circulatory system</a>. They seem to make use of particularly strong venomotion, a rhythmic contraction of <a href="/wiki/Vein" title="Vein">venous</a> wall muscles. In most mammals, the walls of the veins provide mainly passive resistance, maintaining their shape as deoxygenated blood flows through them, but in bats they appear to actively support blood flow back to the heart with this pumping action.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Since their bodies are relatively small and lightweight, bats are not at risk of blood flow rushing to their heads when roosting.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Bats possess a highly adapted <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_system" title="Respiratory system">respiratory system</a> to cope with the demands of powered flight, an energetically taxing activity that requires a large continuous throughput of oxygen. In bats, the relative alveolar surface area and pulmonary capillary blood volume are larger than in most other small quadrupedal mammals.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> During flight the respiratory cycle has a one-to-one relationship with the wing-beat cycle.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Because of the limits of mammalian lungs, bats cannot maintain high-altitude flight.<sup id="cite_ref-pennycuick_57-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pennycuick-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Flying_fox_at_botanical_gardens_in_Sydney_(cropped).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Flying_fox_at_botanical_gardens_in_Sydney_%28cropped%29.jpg/250px-Flying_fox_at_botanical_gardens_in_Sydney_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="319" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Flying_fox_at_botanical_gardens_in_Sydney_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Flying_fox_at_botanical_gardens_in_Sydney_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Flying_fox_at_botanical_gardens_in_Sydney_%28cropped%29.jpg/500px-Flying_fox_at_botanical_gardens_in_Sydney_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1256" data-file-height="2360" /></a><figcaption>The wings are highly vascularized membranes, the larger blood vessels visible against the light.<sup id="cite_ref-benhamo_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-benhamo-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>It takes a lot of energy and an efficient circulatory system to work the flight muscles of bats. Energy supply to the muscles engaged in flight requires about double the amount compared to the muscles that do not use flight as a means of mammalian locomotion. In parallel to energy consumption, blood oxygen levels of flying animals are twice as much as those of their terrestrially locomoting mammals. As the blood supply controls the amount of oxygen supplied throughout the body, the circulatory system must respond accordingly. Therefore, compared to a terrestrial mammal of the same relative size, the bat's <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a> can be up to three times larger, and pump more blood.<sup id="cite_ref-Jürgens_Bartels_Bartels_1981_pp._243–260_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jürgens_Bartels_Bartels_1981_pp._243–260-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Cardiac output is directly derived from heart rate and <a href="/wiki/Stroke_volume" title="Stroke volume">stroke volume</a> of the blood;<sup id="cite_ref-Martini_2015_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martini_2015-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> an active microbat can reach a heart rate of 1000 <a href="/wiki/Beats_per_minute" class="mw-redirect" title="Beats per minute">beats per minute</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WANG2009_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WANG2009-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>With its extremely thin membranous tissue, a bat's wing can significantly contribute to the organism's total gas exchange efficiency.<sup id="cite_ref-Makanya2007_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Makanya2007-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Because of the high energy demand of flight, the bat's body meets those demands by exchanging gas through the patagium of the wing. When the bat has its wings spread it allows for an increase in surface area to volume ratio. The surface area of the wings is about 85% of the total body surface area, suggesting the possibility of a useful degree of gas exchange.<sup id="cite_ref-Makanya2007_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Makanya2007-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The subcutaneous vessels in the membrane lie very close to the surface and allow for the diffusion of oxygen and carbon dioxide.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Digestion" title="Digestion">digestive system</a> of bats has varying adaptations depending on the species of bat and its diet. As in other flying animals, food is processed quickly and effectively to keep up with the energy demand. Insectivorous bats may have certain <a href="/wiki/Digestive_enzyme" title="Digestive enzyme">digestive enzymes</a> to better process insects, such as <a href="/wiki/Chitinase" title="Chitinase">chitinase</a> to break down <a href="/wiki/Chitin" title="Chitin">chitin</a>, which is a large component of insects.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Vampire bats, probably due to their diet of blood, are the only vertebrates that do not have the enzyme <a href="/wiki/Maltase" title="Maltase">maltase</a>, which breaks down <a href="/wiki/Malt_sugar" class="mw-redirect" title="Malt sugar">malt sugar</a>, in their intestinal tract. Nectivorous and frugivorous bats have more maltase and <a href="/wiki/Sucrase" title="Sucrase">sucrase</a> enzymes than insectivorous, to cope with the higher sugar contents of their diet.<sup id="cite_ref-schondube01_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schondube01-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The adaptations of the <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">kidneys</a> of bats vary with their diets. Carnivorous and vampire bats consume large amounts of protein and can output concentrated <a href="/wiki/Urine" title="Urine">urine</a>; their kidneys have a thin cortex and long <a href="/wiki/Renal_papilla" class="mw-redirect" title="Renal papilla">renal papillae</a>. Frugivorous bats lack that ability and have kidneys adapted for <a href="/wiki/Electrolyte" title="Electrolyte">electrolyte</a>-retention due to their low-electrolyte diet; their kidneys accordingly have a thick cortex and very short conical papillae.<sup id="cite_ref-schondube01_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schondube01-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Bats have higher metabolic rates associated with flying, which lead to an increased respiratory water loss. Their large wings are composed of the highly vascularized membranes, increasing the surface area, and leading to <a href="/wiki/Cutaneous" class="mw-redirect" title="Cutaneous">cutaneous</a> evaporative water loss.<sup id="cite_ref-benhamo_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-benhamo-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Water helps maintain their <a href="/wiki/Electrolyte_imbalance" title="Electrolyte imbalance">ionic balance</a> in their blood, <a href="/wiki/Thermoregulation" title="Thermoregulation">thermoregulation</a> system, and removal of wastes and toxins from the body via urine. They are also susceptible to <a href="/wiki/Blood_urea_nitrogen" title="Blood urea nitrogen">blood urea</a> poisoning if they do not receive enough fluid.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The structure of the uterine system in female bats can vary by species, with some having two <a href="/wiki/Uterine_horn" class="mw-redirect" title="Uterine horn">uterine horns</a> while others have a single mainline chamber.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015164_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015164-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Senses">Senses</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Senses"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Echolocation">Echolocation</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Echolocation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Animal_echolocation#Bats" title="Animal echolocation">Animal echolocation §&#160;Bats</a></div>

</div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1235681985" /><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096940132" /><div class="side-box side-box-right listen noprint"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1126788409" />
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><figure class="mw-halign-center" typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/60px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="50" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/120px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></span><figcaption></figcaption></figure></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist"><div class="haudio">
<div class="listen-file-header"><a href="/wiki/File:Bat_feeding_buzz.wav" title="File:Bat feeding buzz.wav">Feeding buzz</a></div>
<div><span typeof="mw:File"><span><audio id="mwe_player_2" controls="" preload="none" data-mw-tmh="" class="mw-file-element" width="232" style="width:232px;" data-durationhint="8" data-mwtitle="Bat_feeding_buzz.wav" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/e/e7/Bat_feeding_buzz.wav/Bat_feeding_buzz.wav.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-transcodekey="ogg" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/e/e7/Bat_feeding_buzz.wav/Bat_feeding_buzz.wav.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e7/Bat_feeding_buzz.wav" type="audio/wav" data-width="0" data-height="0" /></audio></span></span></div>
<div class="description">Time-expanded recording of the feeding buzz of a bat homing in on its prey</div></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-abovebelow"><hr /><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></div>
</div><p>
Microbats and a few megabats emit ultrasonic sounds to produce echoes. Sound intensity of these echos are dependent on subglottic pressure. The bats' cricothyroid muscle controls the orientation pulse frequency, which is an important function. This muscle is located inside the larynx and it is the only tensor muscle capable of aiding phonation.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By comparing the outgoing pulse with the returning echoes, bats can gather information on their surroundings. This allows them to detect prey in darkness.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons201582–84_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons201582–84-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some bat calls can reach 140 <a href="/wiki/Decibels" class="mw-redirect" title="Decibels">decibels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Microbats use their <a href="/wiki/Larynx" title="Larynx">larynx</a> to emit echolocation signals through the mouth or the nose.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Microbat calls range in frequency from 14,000 to well over 100,000&#160;Hz, extending well beyond the range of human hearing (between 20 and 20,000&#160;Hz).<sup id="cite_ref-muller2004_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-muller2004-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Various groups of bats have evolved fleshy extensions around and above the nostrils, known as <a href="/wiki/Nose-leaf" title="Nose-leaf">nose-leaves</a>, which play a role in sound transmission.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons201531_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons201531-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p><figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Animal_echolocation.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Animal_echolocation.svg/290px-Animal_echolocation.svg.png" decoding="async" width="290" height="223" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Animal_echolocation.svg/435px-Animal_echolocation.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Animal_echolocation.svg/580px-Animal_echolocation.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="318" data-file-height="245" /></a><figcaption>Principle of bat echolocation: orange is the call and green is the echo.</figcaption></figure>
<p>In low-duty cycle echolocation, bats can separate their calls and returning echoes by time. They have to time their short calls to finish before echoes return.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones2007_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2007-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The delay of the returning echoes allows the bat to estimate the range to their prey.<sup id="cite_ref-muller2004_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-muller2004-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In high-duty cycle echolocation, bats emit a continuous call and separate pulse and echo in frequency using the <a href="/wiki/Doppler_effect" title="Doppler effect">Doppler effect</a> of their motion in flight. The shift of the returning echoes yields information relating to the motion and location of the bat's prey. These bats must deal with changes in the Doppler shift due to changes in their flight speed. They have adapted to change their pulse emission frequency in relation to their flight speed so echoes still return in the optimal hearing range.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones2007_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2007-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to echolocating prey, bat ears are sensitive to sounds made by their prey, such as the fluttering of moth wings. The complex geometry of ridges on the inner surface of bat ears helps to sharply focus echolocation signals, and to passively listen for any other sound produced by the prey. These ridges can be regarded as the acoustic equivalent of a <a href="/wiki/Fresnel_lens" title="Fresnel lens">Fresnel lens</a>, and exist in a large variety of unrelated animals, such as the <a href="/wiki/Aye-aye" title="Aye-aye">aye-aye</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lesser_galago" class="mw-redirect" title="Lesser galago">lesser galago</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bat-eared_fox" title="Bat-eared fox">bat-eared fox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mouse_lemur" title="Mouse lemur">mouse lemur</a>, and others.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Bats can estimate the elevation of their target using the <a href="/wiki/Interference_pattern" class="mw-redirect" title="Interference pattern">interference patterns</a> from the echoes reflecting from the <a href="/wiki/Tragus_(ear)" title="Tragus (ear)">tragus</a>, a flap of skin in the external ear.<sup id="cite_ref-muller2004_93-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-muller2004-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
