<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Bertholdiatrigona.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Bertholdiatrigona.jpg/250px-Bertholdiatrigona.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="169" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Bertholdiatrigona.jpg/330px-Bertholdiatrigona.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Bertholdiatrigona.jpg/500px-Bertholdiatrigona.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2664" data-file-height="2046" /></a><figcaption>The <a href="/wiki/Bertholdia_trigona" title="Bertholdia trigona">tiger moth</a> (<i>Bertholdia trigona</i>) can <a href="/wiki/Echolocation_jamming" title="Echolocation jamming">jam</a> bat echolocation.<sup id="cite_ref-corcoran2009_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-corcoran2009-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hristov2005_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hristov2005-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>By repeated scanning, bats can mentally construct an accurate image of the environment in which they are moving and of their prey.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some species of moth have exploited this, such as the <a href="/wiki/Arctiidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Arctiidae">tiger moths</a>, which produces <a href="/wiki/Aposematic" class="mw-redirect" title="Aposematic">aposematic</a> ultrasound signals to warn bats that they are chemically protected and therefore distasteful.<sup id="cite_ref-corcoran2009_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-corcoran2009-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hristov2005_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hristov2005-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Moth species including the tiger moth can produce signals to <a href="/wiki/Echolocation_jamming" title="Echolocation jamming">jam bat echolocation</a>. Many moth species have a hearing organ called a <a href="/wiki/Tympanal_organ" title="Tympanal organ">tympanum</a>, which responds to an incoming bat signal by causing the moth's flight muscles to twitch erratically, sending the moth into random evasive manoeuvres.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Vision">Vision</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Vision"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The eyes of most microbat species are small and poorly developed, leading to poor <a href="/wiki/Visual_acuity" title="Visual acuity">visual acuity</a>, but no species is blind.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Most microbats have <a href="/wiki/Mesopic_vision" title="Mesopic vision">mesopic vision</a>, meaning that they can detect light only in low levels, whereas other mammals have <a href="/wiki/Photopic_vision" title="Photopic vision">photopic vision</a>, which allows colour vision. Microbats may use their vision for orientation and while travelling between their roosting grounds and feeding grounds, as echolocation is effective only over short distances. Some species can detect <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> (UV). As the bodies of some microbats have distinct coloration, they may be able to discriminate colours.<sup id="cite_ref-molecule_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-molecule-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wang2004_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wang2004-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Megabat species often have eyesight as good as, if not better than, human vision. Their eyesight is adapted to both night and daylight vision, including some colour vision.<sup id="cite_ref-wang2004_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wang2004-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Magnetoreception">Magnetoreception</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Magnetoreception"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Microbats make use of <a href="/wiki/Magnetoreception" title="Magnetoreception">magnetoreception</a>, in that they have a high sensitivity to the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth&#39;s magnetic field">Earth's magnetic field</a>, as birds do. Microbats use a polarity-based compass, meaning that they differentiate north from south, unlike birds, which use the strength of the magnetic field to differentiate <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitudes</a>, which may be used in long-distance travel. The mechanism is unknown but may involve <a href="/wiki/Magnetite" title="Magnetite">magnetite</a> particles.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Thermoregulation">Thermoregulation</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Thermoregulation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_bat.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Wiki_bat.jpg/330px-Wiki_bat.jpg" decoding="async" width="260" height="154" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Wiki_bat.jpg/390px-Wiki_bat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/05/Wiki_bat.jpg 2x" data-file-width="404" data-file-height="240" /></a><figcaption>Thermographic image of a bat using trapped air as insulation</figcaption></figure>
<p>Most bats are <a href="/wiki/Homeothermic" class="mw-redirect" title="Homeothermic">homeothermic</a> (having a stable body temperature), the exception being the vesper bats (Vespertilionidae), the horseshoe bats (Rhinolophidae), the free-tailed bats (Molossidae), and the bent-winged bats (Miniopteridae), which extensively use <a href="/wiki/Heterothermy" title="Heterothermy">heterothermy</a> (where body temperature can vary).<sup id="cite_ref-Nowack_2017_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nowack_2017-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>112<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Compared to other mammals, bats have a high <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a>. The wings are filled with blood vessels, and lose body heat when extended. At rest, they may wrap their wings around themselves to trap a layer of warm air. Smaller bats generally have a higher metabolic rate than larger bats, and so need to consume more food in order to maintain homeothermy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAltringham201199–100_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAltringham201199–100-114"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>113<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Bats may avoid flying during the day to prevent overheating in the sun, since their dark wing-membranes absorb solar radiation. Bats may not be able to dissipate heat if the ambient temperature is too high;<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>114<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> they use saliva to cool themselves in extreme conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-pennycuick_57-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pennycuick-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Among megabats, the flying fox <i><a href="/wiki/Pteropus_hypomelanus" class="mw-redirect" title="Pteropus hypomelanus">Pteropus hypomelanus</a></i> uses saliva and wing-fanning to cool itself while roosting during the hottest part of the day.<sup id="cite_ref-Ochoa-AcuñaKunz1999_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ochoa-AcuñaKunz1999-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>115<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Among microbats, the <a href="/wiki/Yuma_myotis" title="Yuma myotis">Yuma myotis</a> (<i>Myotis yumanensis</i>), the Mexican free-tailed bat, and the <a href="/wiki/Pallid_bat" title="Pallid bat">pallid bat</a> (<i>Antrozous pallidus</i>) cope with temperatures up to 45&#160;°C (113&#160;°F) by panting, salivating, and licking their fur to promote evaporative cooling; this is sufficient to dissipate twice their metabolic heat production.<sup id="cite_ref-LichtLeitner1967_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LichtLeitner1967-117"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>116<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Bats also possess a system of <a href="/wiki/Sphincter" title="Sphincter">sphincter</a> valves on the arterial side of the vascular network that runs along the edge of their wings. When fully open, these allow oxygenated blood to flow through the <a href="/wiki/Capillary" title="Capillary">capillary</a> network across the wing membrane; when contracted, they <a href="/wiki/Shunt_(medical)" title="Shunt (medical)">shunt</a> flow directly to the veins, bypassing the wing capillaries. This allows bats to control how much heat is exchanged through the flight membrane, allowing them to release heat during flight. Many other mammals use the capillary network in oversized ears for the same purpose.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>117<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Torpor">Torpor</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Torpor"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Tri-colored_bat_in_torpor.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Tri-colored_bat_in_torpor.JPG/250px-Tri-colored_bat_in_torpor.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="239" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Tri-colored_bat_in_torpor.JPG/330px-Tri-colored_bat_in_torpor.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Tri-colored_bat_in_torpor.JPG/500px-Tri-colored_bat_in_torpor.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1408" data-file-height="1980" /></a><figcaption>A <a href="/wiki/Tricoloured_bat" class="mw-redirect" title="Tricoloured bat">tricoloured bat</a> (<i>Perimyotis subflavus</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Torpor" title="Torpor">torpor</a></figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Torpor" title="Torpor">Torpor</a>, a state of decreased activity where the body temperature and <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a> decreases, is especially useful for bats, as they use a large amount of energy while active, depend upon an unreliable food source, and have a limited ability to store fat. They generally drop their body temperature in this state to 6–30&#160;°C (43–86&#160;°F), and may reduce their energy expenditure by 50 to 99%.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>118<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Tropical bats may use it to avoid predation, by reducing the amount of time spent on foraging and thus reducing the chance of being caught by a predator.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>119<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Megabats were generally believed to be homeothermic, but three species of small megabats, with a mass of about <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1154941027">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);clip-path:polygon(0px 0px,0px 0px,0px 0px);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style>50 grams (<span class="frac">1<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">3</span>&#8260;<span class="den">4</span></span> ounces), have been known to use torpor: the <a href="/wiki/Common_blossom_bat" title="Common blossom bat">common blossom bat</a> (<i>Syconycteris australis</i>), the <a href="/wiki/Long-tongued_nectar_bat" title="Long-tongued nectar bat">long-tongued nectar bat</a> (<i>Macroglossus minimus</i>), and the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_tube-nosed_bat" title="Eastern tube-nosed bat">eastern tube-nosed bat</a> (<i>Nyctimene robinsoni</i>). Torpid states last longer in the summer for megabats than in the winter.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>120<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>During <a href="/wiki/Hibernation" title="Hibernation">hibernation</a>, bats enter a torpid state and decrease their body temperature for 99.6% of their hibernation period; even during periods of arousal, when they return their body temperature to normal, they sometimes enter a shallow torpid state, known as "heterothermic arousal".<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>121<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some bats <a href="/wiki/Aestivation" title="Aestivation">become dormant during higher temperatures</a> to keep cool in the summer months.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>122<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Heterothermic bats during long migrations may fly at night and go into a torpid state roosting in the daytime. Unlike migratory birds, which fly during the day and feed during the night, nocturnal bats have a conflict between travelling and eating. The energy saved reduces their need to feed, and also decreases the duration of migration, which may prevent them from spending too much time in unfamiliar places, and decrease predation. In some species, pregnant individuals may not use torpor.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>123<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>124<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Scientists reported in January 2025 that they had discovered how bats travel hundreds of miles in the spring to give birth in warmer temperatures: they surf storm fronts.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>125<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Size">Size</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Size"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The smallest bat is <a href="/wiki/Kitti%27s_hog-nosed_bat" title="Kitti&#39;s hog-nosed bat">Kitti's hog-nosed bat</a> (<i>Craseonycteris thonglongyai</i>), which is <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1154941027" />29–34&#160;mm (<span class="frac">1<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">8</span></span>–<span class="frac">1<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">3</span>&#8260;<span class="den">8</span></span>&#160;in) long with a 150-millimetre (6&#160;in) wingspan and weighs <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1154941027" />2–2.6&#160;g (<span class="frac"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">16</span></span>–<span class="frac"><span class="num">3</span>&#8260;<span class="den">32</span></span>&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Edge_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edge-127"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>126<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is also arguably the smallest <a href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" class="mw-redirect" title="Extant taxon">extant</a> species of mammal, next to the <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_shrew" title="Etruscan shrew">Etruscan shrew</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>127<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The largest bats are a few species of <i><a href="/wiki/Pteropus" title="Pteropus">Pteropus</a></i> megabats and the <a href="/wiki/Giant_golden-crowned_flying_fox" title="Giant golden-crowned flying fox">giant golden-crowned flying fox</a>, (<i>Acerodon jubatus</i>), which can weigh <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1154941027" />1.6&#160;kg (<span class="frac">3<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">2</span></span>&#160;lb) with a wingspan of 1.7&#160;m (5&#160;ft 7&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>128<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Larger bats tend to use lower frequencies and smaller bats higher for echolocation; high-frequency echolocation is better at detecting smaller prey. Small prey may be absent in the diets of large bats as they are unable to detect them.<sup id="cite_ref-gonsalves_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gonsalves-130"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>129<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The adaptations of a particular bat species can directly influence what kinds of prey are available to it.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>130<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Bats are thought to contribute less than 10% of the total biomass of wild terrestrial mammals, but about 66% of all mammalian individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>131<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Ecology">Ecology</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Ecology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Common_tent-making_bats.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Common_tent-making_bats.JPG/250px-Common_tent-making_bats.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Common_tent-making_bats.JPG/330px-Common_tent-making_bats.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Common_tent-making_bats.JPG/500px-Common_tent-making_bats.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2570" data-file-height="1713" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Tent-making_bat" title="Tent-making bat">Tent-making bats</a> (<i>Uroderma bilobatum</i>) in Costa Rica</figcaption></figure>
<p>Flight has enabled bats to become one of the most widely distributed groups of mammals.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>132<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Apart from the Arctic, the Antarctic and a few isolated oceanic islands, bats exist in almost every habitat on Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>133<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Tropical areas tend to have more species than temperate ones.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons201532_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons201532-135"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>134<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Different species select different habitats during different seasons, ranging from seasides to mountains and deserts, but they require suitable roosts. Bat roosts can be found in hollows, crevices, foliage, and even human-made structures, and include "tents" the bats construct with leaves.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>135<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Megabats generally roost in trees.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAltringham201121_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAltringham201121-137"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>136<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Most microbats are <a href="/wiki/Nocturnality" title="Nocturnality">nocturnal</a><sup id="cite_ref-bats_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bats-138"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>137<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and megabats are typically <a href="/wiki/Diurnal_cycle" title="Diurnal cycle">diurnal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Crepuscular" class="mw-redirect" title="Crepuscular">crepuscular</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Schwab_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schwab-139"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>138<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>139<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Microbats are known to exhibit diurnal behaviour in temperate regions during summer when there is insufficient night time to forage,<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>140<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>141<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and in areas where there are few avian predators during the day.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>142<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>143<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In temperate areas, some microbats <a href="/wiki/Animal_migration" title="Animal migration">migrate</a> hundreds of kilometres to winter hibernation dens;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFenton200160–62_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFenton200160–62-145"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>144<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> others pass into torpor in cold weather, rousing and feeding when warm weather allows insects to be active.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFenton200193–94_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFenton200193–94-146"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>145<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Others retreat to caves for winter and hibernate for as much as six months.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFenton200193–94_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFenton200193–94-146"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>145<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Microbats rarely fly in rain; it interferes with their echolocation, and they are unable to hunt.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>146<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Food_and_feeding">Food and feeding</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Food and feeding"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><span><video id="mwe_player_3" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Microbats%27_hunting.ogv/220px--Microbats%27_hunting.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" data-mw-tmh="" class="mw-file-element" width="220" height="165" data-durationhint="61" data-mwtitle="Microbats&#39;_hunting.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" resource="/wiki/File:Microbats%27_hunting.ogv"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/24/Microbats%27_hunting.ogv/Microbats%27_hunting.ogv.480p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="480p.vp9.webm" data-width="640" data-height="480" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/24/Microbats%27_hunting.ogv" type="video/ogg; codecs=&quot;theora, flac&quot;" data-width="640" data-height="480" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/24/Microbats%27_hunting.ogv/Microbats%27_hunting.ogv.144p.mjpeg.mov" type="video/quicktime" data-transcodekey="144p.mjpeg.mov" data-width="192" data-height="144" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/24/Microbats%27_hunting.ogv/Microbats%27_hunting.ogv.240p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="240p.vp9.webm" data-width="320" data-height="240" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/24/Microbats%27_hunting.ogv/Microbats%27_hunting.ogv.360p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="360p.vp9.webm" data-width="480" data-height="360" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/24/Microbats%27_hunting.ogv/Microbats%27_hunting.ogv.360p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp8, vorbis&quot;" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="480" data-height="360" /></video></span><figcaption>Bats feeding on insects over a lake</figcaption></figure>
<p>Different bat species have different diets, including insects, nectar, pollen, fruit and even vertebrates.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015104–107_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015104–107-148"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>147<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Megabats are mostly fruit, nectar and pollen eaters.<sup id="cite_ref-Schwab_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schwab-139"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>138<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Due to their small size, high-metabolism and rapid burning of energy through flight, bats must consume large amounts of food for their size. Insectivorous bats may eat over 120 percent of their body weight per day, while frugivorous bats may eat over twice their weight.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015116_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015116-149"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>148<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They can travel significant distances each night, exceptionally as much as 38.5&#160;km (24&#160;mi) in the spotted bat (<i><a href="/wiki/Euderma_maculatum" class="mw-redirect" title="Euderma maculatum">Euderma maculatum</a></i>), in search of food.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>149<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Bats use a variety of hunting strategies.<sup id="cite_ref-gonsalves_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gonsalves-130"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>129<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Bats get most of their water from the food they eat; many species also drink from water sources like lakes and streams, flying over the surface and dipping their tongues into the water.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons201576_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons201576-151"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>150<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The Chiroptera as a whole are in the process of losing the ability to synthesise <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">vitamin C</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid22069493_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22069493-152"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>151<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In a test of 34 bat species from six major families, including major insect- and fruit-eating bat families, all were found to have lost the ability to synthesise it, and this loss may derive from a common bat ancestor, as a single mutation.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>152<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>b<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> At least two species of bat, the frugivorous bat (<i>Rousettus leschenaultii</i>) and the insectivorous bat (<i>Hipposideros armiger</i>), have retained their ability to produce vitamin C.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid21037206_154-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21037206-154"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>153<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Insects">Insects</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Insects"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Most microbats, especially in temperate areas, prey on insects.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015104–107_148-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015104–107-148"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>147<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The diet of an insectivorous bat may span many species,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015108–110_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015108–110-156"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>154<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> including <a href="/wiki/Flies" class="mw-redirect" title="Flies">flies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mosquito" title="Mosquito">mosquitos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beetle" title="Beetle">beetles</a>, moths, <a href="/wiki/Grasshopper" title="Grasshopper">grasshoppers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cricket_(insect)" title="Cricket (insect)">crickets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Termite" title="Termite">termites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bee" title="Bee">bees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wasp" title="Wasp">wasps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mayflies" class="mw-redirect" title="Mayflies">mayflies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caddisflies" class="mw-redirect" title="Caddisflies">caddisflies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MacDonald_48-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wray_Jusino_Banik_Palmer_2018_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wray_Jusino_Banik_Palmer_2018-157"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>155<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>156<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Large numbers of Mexican free-tailed bats (<i>Tadarida brasiliensis</i>) fly hundreds of metres above the ground in central Texas to feed on migrating moths.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>157<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Species that hunt insects in flight, like the <a href="/wiki/Little_brown_bat" title="Little brown bat">little brown bat</a> (<i>Myotis lucifugus</i>), may catch an insect in mid-air with the mouth, and eat it in the air or use their tail membranes or wings to scoop up the insect and carry it to the mouth.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>158<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015120_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015120-161"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>159<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The bat may also take the insect back to its roost and eat it there.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>160<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Slower moving bat species, such as the <a href="/wiki/Brown_long-eared_bat" title="Brown long-eared bat">brown long-eared bat</a> (<i>Plecotus auritus</i>) and many horseshoe bat species, may take or <a href="/wiki/Bat_flight#Gleaning" title="Bat flight">glean</a> insects from vegetation or hunt them from perches.<sup id="cite_ref-MacDonald_48-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Insectivorous bats living at high latitudes have to consume prey with higher energetic value than tropical bats.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>161<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Fruit_and_nectar">Fruit and nectar</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Fruit and nectar"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Rousettus_aegyptiacus.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Rousettus_aegyptiacus.jpg/250px-Rousettus_aegyptiacus.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="169" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Rousettus_aegyptiacus.jpg/330px-Rousettus_aegyptiacus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Rousettus_aegyptiacus.jpg/500px-Rousettus_aegyptiacus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1300" data-file-height="1000" /></a><figcaption>An <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_fruit_bat" title="Egyptian fruit bat">Egyptian fruit bat</a> (<i>Rousettus aegyptiacus</i>) carrying a <a href="/wiki/Ficus" title="Ficus">fig</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Fruit eating, or frugivory, is found in both major suborders. Bats prefer ripe fruit, pulling it off the trees with their teeth. They fly back to their roosts to eat the fruit, sucking out the juice and spitting the seeds and pulp out onto the ground. This helps disperse the seeds of these fruit trees, which may take root and grow where the bats have left them, and many species of plants depend on bats for <a href="/wiki/Seed_dispersal" title="Seed dispersal">seed dispersal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>162<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015115_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015115-165"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>163<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Jamaican_fruit_bat" title="Jamaican fruit bat">Jamaican fruit bat</a> (<i>Artibeus jamaicensis</i>) has been recorded carrying fruits weighing <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1154941027" />3–14&#160;g (<span class="frac"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">8</span></span>–<span class="frac"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">2</span></span>&#160;oz) or even as much as <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1154941027" />50&#160;g (<span class="frac">1<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">3</span>&#8260;<span class="den">4</span></span>&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Ortega2001_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ortega2001-166"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>164<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Nectar-eating bats have acquired specialised adaptations. These bats possess long muzzles and long, extensible <a href="/wiki/Tongue" title="Tongue">tongues</a> covered in fine bristles that aid them in feeding on particular flowers and plants.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015115_165-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015115-165"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>163<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NGtongue_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NGtongue-167"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>165<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tube-lipped_nectar_bat" title="Tube-lipped nectar bat">tube-lipped nectar bat</a> (<i>Anoura fistulata</i>) has the longest tongue of any mammal relative to its body size. This is beneficial to them in terms of pollination and feeding. Their long, narrow tongues can reach deep into the long cup shape of some flowers. When the tongue retracts, it coils up inside the rib cage.<sup id="cite_ref-NGtongue_167-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NGtongue-167"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>165<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Because of these features, nectar-feeding bats cannot easily turn to other food sources in times of scarcity, making them more prone to extinction than other types of bat.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>166<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>167<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Nectar feeding also aids a variety of plants, since these bats serve as <a href="/wiki/Pollinator" title="Pollinator">pollinators</a>, as pollen gets attached to their fur while they are feeding. Around 500 species of flowering plant rely on bat pollination and thus tend to open their flowers at night.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015115_165-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015115-165"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>163<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Many rainforest plants depend on bat pollination.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>168<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Vertebrates">Vertebrates</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Vertebrates"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:GreaterNoctule.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/GreaterNoctule.JPG/250px-GreaterNoctule.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="190" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/GreaterNoctule.JPG/330px-GreaterNoctule.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/GreaterNoctule.JPG/500px-GreaterNoctule.JPG 2x" data-file-width="638" data-file-height="712" /></a><figcaption>The <a href="/wiki/Greater_noctule_bat" title="Greater noctule bat">greater noctule bat</a> (<i>Nyctalus lasiopterus</i>) uses its large teeth to catch birds.<sup id="cite_ref-popa_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-popa-171"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>169<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>Some bats prey on other vertebrates, such as fish, frogs, lizards, birds and mammals.<sup id="cite_ref-MacDonald_48-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015107_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015107-172"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>170<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Fringe-lipped_bat" title="Fringe-lipped bat">fringe-lipped bat</a> (<i>Trachops cirrhosus,</i>) for example, is skilled at catching frogs. These bats locate large groups of frogs by tracking their mating calls, then plucking them from the surface of the water with their sharp canine teeth.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>171<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Greater_noctule_bat" title="Greater noctule bat">greater noctule bat</a> can catch birds in flight.<sup id="cite_ref-popa_171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-popa-171"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>169<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some species, like the greater bulldog bat (<i><a href="/wiki/Noctilio_leporinus" class="mw-redirect" title="Noctilio leporinus">Noctilio leporinus</a></i>) hunt fish. They use echolocation to detect small ripples on the water's surface, swoop down and use specially enlarged claws on their hind feet to grab the fish, then take their prey to a feeding roost and consume it.<sup id="cite_ref-Schnitzler_1994_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schnitzler_1994-174"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>172<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> At least two species of bat are known to feed on other bats: the <a href="/wiki/Spectral_bat" title="Spectral bat">spectral bat</a> (<i>Vampyrum spectrum</i>), and the <a href="/wiki/Ghost_bat" title="Ghost bat">ghost bat</a> (<i>Macroderma gigas</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFenton20014–5_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFenton20014–5-175"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>173<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Blood">Blood</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Blood"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Desmo-boden_(cropped).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Desmo-boden_%28cropped%29.jpg/250px-Desmo-boden_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="160" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Desmo-boden_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Desmo-boden_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Desmo-boden_%28cropped%29.jpg/500px-Desmo-boden_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1403" data-file-height="1020" /></a><figcaption>The <a href="/wiki/Common_vampire_bat" title="Common vampire bat">common vampire bat</a> (<i>Desmodus rotundus</i>) feeds on blood (<a href="/wiki/Hematophagy" title="Hematophagy">hematophagy</a>).</figcaption></figure>
<p>A few species, specifically the common, <a href="/wiki/White-winged_vampire_bat" title="White-winged vampire bat">white-winged</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hairy-legged_vampire_bat" title="Hairy-legged vampire bat">hairy-legged</a> vampire bats, feed only on animal blood (<a href="/wiki/Hematophagy" title="Hematophagy">hematophagy</a>). The common vampire bat typically feeds on large mammals such as <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a>; the hairy-legged and white-winged vampires feed on birds.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>174<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Vampire bats target sleeping prey and can detect deep breathing.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015119_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015119-177"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>175<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Heat sensors in the nose help them to detect blood vessels near the surface of the skin.<sup id="cite_ref-Wilkinson_1990_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilkinson_1990-178"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>176<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They pierce the animal's skin with their teeth, biting away a small flap,<sup id="cite_ref-Nowak_1991_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nowak_1991-179"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>177<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and lap up the blood with their tongues, which have lateral grooves adapted to this purpose.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>178<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The blood is kept from clotting by an <a href="/wiki/Anticoagulant" title="Anticoagulant">anticoagulant</a> in the saliva.<sup id="cite_ref-Nowak_1991_179-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nowak_1991-179"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>177<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Predators,_parasites,_and_diseases"><span id="Predators.2C_parasites.2C_and_diseases"></span>Predators, parasites, and diseases</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Predators, parasites, and diseases"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Bat_virome" title="Bat virome">Bat virome</a></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Predation">Predation</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Predation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Bats are subject to predation from <a href="/wiki/Birds_of_prey" class="mw-redirect" title="Birds of prey">birds of prey</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Owls" class="mw-redirect" title="Owls">owls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hawks" class="mw-redirect" title="Hawks">hawks</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Falcons" class="mw-redirect" title="Falcons">falcons</a>, and at roosts from terrestrial predators able to climb, such as cats.<sup id="cite_ref-RydellSpeakman1995_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RydellSpeakman1995-181"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>179<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Low-flying bats are vulnerable to <a href="/wiki/Crocodile" title="Crocodile">crocodiles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>180<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Twenty species of tropical New World <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">snakes</a> are known to capture bats, often waiting at the entrances of refuges, such as caves, for bats to fly past.<sup id="cite_ref-EsbérardVrcibradic2007_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EsbérardVrcibradic2007-183"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>181<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> J. Rydell and J. R. Speakman argue that bats evolved nocturnality during the early and middle <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a> period to avoid predators.<sup id="cite_ref-RydellSpeakman1995_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RydellSpeakman1995-181"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>179<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The evidence is thought by some zoologists to be equivocal so far.<sup id="cite_ref-LimaO&#39;Keefe2013_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LimaO&#39;Keefe2013-184"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>182<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Little_Brown_Bat_with_White_Nose_Syndrome_(Greeley_Mine,_cropped).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Little_Brown_Bat_with_White_Nose_Syndrome_%28Greeley_Mine%2C_cropped%29.jpg/250px-Little_Brown_Bat_with_White_Nose_Syndrome_%28Greeley_Mine%2C_cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="200" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Little_Brown_Bat_with_White_Nose_Syndrome_%28Greeley_Mine%2C_cropped%29.jpg/330px-Little_Brown_Bat_with_White_Nose_Syndrome_%28Greeley_Mine%2C_cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Little_Brown_Bat_with_White_Nose_Syndrome_%28Greeley_Mine%2C_cropped%29.jpg/500px-Little_Brown_Bat_with_White_Nose_Syndrome_%28Greeley_Mine%2C_cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1216" data-file-height="1432" /></a><figcaption>A <a href="/wiki/Little_brown_bat" title="Little brown bat">little brown bat</a> with <a href="/wiki/White_nose_syndrome" class="mw-redirect" title="White nose syndrome">white nose syndrome</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>As are most mammals, bats are hosts to a number of internal and external parasites.<sup id="cite_ref-Léger2020_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Léger2020-185"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>183<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Among <a href="/wiki/Ectoparasite" class="mw-redirect" title="Ectoparasite">ectoparasites</a>, bats carry <a href="/wiki/Flea" title="Flea">fleas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mite" title="Mite">mites</a>, as well as specific parasites such as <a href="/wiki/Bat_bug" title="Bat bug">bat bugs</a> and bat flies (<a href="/wiki/Nycteribiidae" title="Nycteribiidae">Nycteribiidae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Streblidae" title="Streblidae">Streblidae</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>184<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clayton2015_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clayton2015-187"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>185<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Bats are among the few non-aquatic mammalian orders that do not host <a href="/wiki/Lice" class="mw-redirect" title="Lice">lice</a>, possibly due to competition from more specialised parasites that occupy the same niche.<sup id="cite_ref-Clayton2015_187-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clayton2015-187"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>185<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/White_nose_syndrome" class="mw-redirect" title="White nose syndrome">White nose syndrome</a> is a condition associated with the deaths of millions of bats in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_United_States" title="Eastern United States">Eastern United States</a> and Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>186<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The disease is named after a white <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungus</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Pseudogymnoascus_destructans" title="Pseudogymnoascus destructans">Pseudogymnoascus destructans</a></i>, found growing on the muzzles, ears, and wings of affected bats. The fungus is mostly spread from bat to bat, and causes the disease.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>187<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The fungus was first discovered in central New York State in 2006 and spread quickly to the entire Eastern US north of Florida; mortality rates of 90–100% have been observed in most affected caves.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>188<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_states" class="mw-redirect" title="Mid-Atlantic states">mid-Atlantic states</a> have, since 2006, witnessed entire species completely extirpated and others with numbers that have gone from the hundreds of thousands, even millions, to a few hundred or less.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>189<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Nova Scotia, Quebec, Ontario, and New Brunswick have witnessed identical die offs, with the Canadian government making preparations to protect all remaining bat populations in its territory.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>190<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Scientific evidence suggests that longer winters where the fungus has a longer period to infect bats result in greater mortality.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>191<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>192<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FrickPollock2010_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FrickPollock2010-195"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>193<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2014, the infection crossed the Mississippi River,<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>194<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and in 2017, it was found on bats in Texas.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>195<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Zoonotic_diseases">Zoonotic diseases</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Zoonotic diseases"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Bats are <a href="/wiki/Natural_reservoir" title="Natural reservoir">natural reservoirs</a> for a large number of <a href="/wiki/Zoonosis" title="Zoonosis">zoonotic</a> <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogens</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Wong2007_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wong2007-198"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>196<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> including <a href="/wiki/Rabies_in_animals#Bats" title="Rabies in animals">rabies</a>, endemic in many bat populations,<sup id="cite_ref-McColl2000_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McColl2000-199"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>197<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Calisher2006_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Calisher2006-200"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>198<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brüssow2012_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brüssow2012-201"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>199<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Histoplasmosis" title="Histoplasmosis">histoplasmosis</a> both directly and in guano,<sup id="cite_ref-CDC2014_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2014-202"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>200<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Henipavirus" title="Henipavirus">Nipah and Hendra viruses</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-EatonBroder2006_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EatonBroder2006-203"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>201<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Halpin2000_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halpin2000-204"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>202<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and possibly the <a href="/wiki/Ebola_virus" class="mw-redirect" title="Ebola virus">ebola virus</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Leroy2005_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leroy2005-205"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>203<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SciAm2006_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SciAm2006-206"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>204<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> whose natural reservoir is yet unknown.<sup id="cite_ref-CDC_EVD_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_EVD-207"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>205<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>206<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Their high mobility, broad distribution, long life spans, substantial <a href="/wiki/Sympatry" title="Sympatry">sympatry</a> (range overlap) of species, and social behaviour make bats favourable hosts and vectors of disease.<sup id="cite_ref-Castro2013_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Castro2013-209"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>207<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Reviews have found different answers as to whether bats have more zoonotic viruses than other mammal groups. One 2015 review found that bats, rodents, and primates all harbored significantly more <a href="/wiki/Zoonotic" class="mw-redirect" title="Zoonotic">zoonotic</a> viruses (which can be transmitted to humans) than other mammal groups, though the differences among the aforementioned three groups were not significant (bats have no more zoonotic viruses than rodents and primates).<sup id="cite_ref-Olival_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Olival-210"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>208<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Another 2020 review of mammals and birds found that the identity of the taxonomic groups did not have any impact on the probability of harboring zoonotic viruses. Instead, more diverse groups had greater viral diversity.<sup id="cite_ref-Mollentze_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mollentze-211"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>209<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>They seem to be highly resistant to many of the pathogens they carry, suggesting a degree of adaptation to their immune systems.<sup id="cite_ref-Castro2013_209-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Castro2013-209"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>207<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>210<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>211<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Their interactions with livestock and pets, including predation by vampire bats, accidental encounters, and the scavenging of bat carcasses, compound the risk of zoonotic transmission.<sup id="cite_ref-Calisher2006_200-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Calisher2006-200"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>198<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Bats are implicated in the emergence of <a href="/wiki/Severe_acute_respiratory_syndrome" class="mw-redirect" title="Severe acute respiratory syndrome">severe acute respiratory syndrome</a> (SARS) in China, since they serve as natural hosts for <a href="/wiki/Coronavirus" title="Coronavirus">coronaviruses</a>, several from a single cave in <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan</a>, one of which developed into the SARS virus.<sup id="cite_ref-CDC2014_202-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2014-202"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>200<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Li2005_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Li2005-214"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>212<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DrostenHu2017_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DrostenHu2017-215"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>213<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, they neither cause nor spread <a href="/wiki/COVID-19" title="COVID-19">COVID-19</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>214<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Behaviour_and_life_history">Behaviour and life history</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Behaviour and life history"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Social_structure">Social structure</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Social structure"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><span><video id="mwe_player_4" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Bracken_Bat_Cave_evening_of_17_June_2017.ogv/220px--Bracken_Bat_Cave_evening_of_17_June_2017.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" data-mw-tmh="" class="mw-file-element" width="220" height="124" data-durationhint="108" data-mwtitle="Bracken_Bat_Cave_evening_of_17_June_2017.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" resource="/wiki/File:Bracken_Bat_Cave_evening_of_17_June_2017.ogv"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/b/ba/Bracken_Bat_Cave_evening_of_17_June_2017.ogv/Bracken_Bat_Cave_evening_of_17_June_2017.ogv.480p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="480p.vp9.webm" data-width="854" data-height="480" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/b/ba/Bracken_Bat_Cave_evening_of_17_June_2017.ogv/Bracken_Bat_Cave_evening_of_17_June_2017.ogv.720p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="720p.vp9.webm" data-width="1280" data-height="720" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/ba/Bracken_Bat_Cave_evening_of_17_June_2017.ogv" type="video/ogg; codecs=&quot;theora, vorbis&quot;" data-width="1280" data-height="720" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/b/ba/Bracken_Bat_Cave_evening_of_17_June_2017.ogv/Bracken_Bat_Cave_evening_of_17_June_2017.ogv.144p.mjpeg.mov" type="video/quicktime" data-transcodekey="144p.mjpeg.mov" data-width="256" data-height="144" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/b/ba/Bracken_Bat_Cave_evening_of_17_June_2017.ogv/Bracken_Bat_Cave_evening_of_17_June_2017.ogv.240p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="240p.vp9.webm" data-width="426" data-height="240" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/b/ba/Bracken_Bat_Cave_evening_of_17_June_2017.ogv/Bracken_Bat_Cave_evening_of_17_June_2017.ogv.360p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp8, vorbis&quot;" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/b/ba/Bracken_Bat_Cave_evening_of_17_June_2017.ogv/Bracken_Bat_Cave_evening_of_17_June_2017.ogv.360p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="360p.vp9.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" /></video></span><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Bracken_Bat_Cave" class="mw-redirect" title="Bracken Bat Cave">Bracken Bat Cave</a>, home to twenty million Mexican free-tailed bats</figcaption></figure>
<p>Some bats lead solitary lives, while others live in colonies of more than a million.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFenton200195–107_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFenton200195–107-217"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>215<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For instance, the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_free-tailed_bat" title="Mexican free-tailed bat">Mexican free-tailed bat</a> fly for more than one thousand miles to the 100-foot (30&#160;m) wide cave known as <a href="/wiki/Bracken_Cave" title="Bracken Cave">Bracken Cave</a> every March to October which plays home to an astonishing twenty million of the species,<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>216<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> whereas a <a href="/wiki/Mouse-eared_bat" title="Mouse-eared bat">mouse-eared bat</a> lives an almost completely solitary life.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>217<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Living in large colonies lessens the risk to an individual of predation.<sup id="cite_ref-MacDonald_48-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Temperate bat species may <a href="/wiki/Swarm_behaviour" title="Swarm behaviour">swarm</a> at hibernation sites as autumn approaches. This may serve to introduce young to hibernation sites, signal reproduction in adults and allow adults to breed with those from other groups.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015188–189_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015188–189-220"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>218<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Several species have a <a href="/wiki/Fission%E2%80%93fusion_society" title="Fission–fusion society">fission-fusion social structure</a>, where large numbers of bats congregate in one roosting area, along with breaking up and mixing of subgroups. Within these societies, bats are able to maintain long-term relationships.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>219<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some of these relationships consist of <a href="/wiki/Matrilineal" class="mw-redirect" title="Matrilineal">matrilineally</a> related females and their dependent offspring.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>220<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Food sharing and <a href="/wiki/Mutual_grooming" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutual grooming">mutual grooming</a> may occur in certain species, such as the common vampire bat (<i>Desmodus rotundus</i>), and these strengthen social bonds.<sup id="cite_ref-Carter_&amp;_Wilkinson_2013_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carter_&amp;_Wilkinson_2013-223"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>221<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wilkinson_1986_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilkinson_1986-224"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>222<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Homosexual fellatio has been observed in the Bonin flying fox <i>Pteropus pselaphon</i><sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>223<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and the Indian flying fox <i>Pteropus medius</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>224<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> though the function and purpose of this behaviour is not clear. 
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Communication">Communication</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Communication"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Journal.pone.0006746.g001.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Journal.pone.0006746.g001.png/250px-Journal.pone.0006746.g001.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="212" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Journal.pone.0006746.g001.png/330px-Journal.pone.0006746.g001.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Journal.pone.0006746.g001.png/500px-Journal.pone.0006746.g001.png 2x" data-file-width="1418" data-file-height="1366" /></a><figcaption>Acoustics of the songs of <a href="/wiki/Mexican_free-tailed_bat" title="Mexican free-tailed bat">Mexican free-tailed bats</a><sup id="cite_ref-Bohn_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bohn-227"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>225<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>Bats are among the most vocal of mammals and produce calls to attract mates, find roost partners and defend resources. These calls are typically low-frequency and can travel long distances.<sup id="cite_ref-MacDonald_48-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015190–194_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015190–194-228"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>226<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Mexican free-tailed bats are one of the few species to "sing" like birds. Males sing to attract females. Songs have three phrases: chirps, trills and buzzes, the former having "A" and "B" syllables. Bat songs are highly stereotypical but with variation in syllable number, phrase order, and phrase repetitions between individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-Bohn_227-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bohn-227"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>225<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Among <a href="/wiki/Greater_spear-nosed_bat" title="Greater spear-nosed bat">greater spear-nosed bats</a> (<i>Phyllostomus hastatus</i>), females produce loud, broadband calls among their roost mates to form group cohesion. Calls differ between roosting groups and may arise from vocal learning.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>227<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In a study on captive Egyptian fruit bats, 70% of the directed calls could be identified by the researchers as to which individual bat made it, and 60% could be categorised into four contexts: squabbling over food, jostling over position in their sleeping cluster, protesting over mating attempts and arguing when perched in close proximity to each other. The animals made slightly different sounds when communicating with different individual bats, especially those of the opposite sex.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>228<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the highly <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexually dimorphic</a> <a href="/wiki/Hammer-headed_bat" title="Hammer-headed bat">hammer-headed bat</a> (<i>Hypsignathus monstrosus</i>), males produce deep, resonating, monotonous calls to attract females. Bats in flight make vocal signals for traffic control. Greater bulldog bats honk when on a collision course with each other.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015190–194_228-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015190–194-228"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>226<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Bats also communicate by other means. Male <a href="/wiki/Little_yellow-shouldered_bat" title="Little yellow-shouldered bat">little yellow-shouldered bats</a> (<i>Sturnira lilium</i>) have shoulder glands that produce a spicy odour during the breeding season. Like many other species, they have hair specialised for retaining and dispersing secretions. Such hair forms a conspicuous collar around the necks of the some Old World megabat males. Male <a href="/wiki/Greater_sac-winged_bat" title="Greater sac-winged bat">greater sac-winged bats</a> (<i>Saccopteryx bilineata</i>) have sacs in their wings in which they mix body secretions like saliva and urine to create a perfume that they sprinkle on roost sites, a behaviour known as "salting". Salting may be accompanied by singing.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015190–194_228-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015190–194-228"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>226<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Reproduction_and_life_cycle">Reproduction and life cycle</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Reproduction and life cycle"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Desmo-kolonie-baum.tif" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Desmo-kolonie-baum.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Desmo-kolonie-baum.tif.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Desmo-kolonie-baum.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Desmo-kolonie-baum.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Desmo-kolonie-baum.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Desmo-kolonie-baum.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1904" data-file-height="1424" /></a><figcaption>Group of polygynous vampire bats</figcaption></figure>
<p>Most bat species are <a href="/wiki/Polygyny_in_animals" title="Polygyny in animals">polygynous</a>, where males mate with multiple females. Male pipistrelle, noctule and vampire bats may claim and defend resources that attract females, such as roost sites, and mate with those females. Males unable to claim a site are forced to live on the periphery where they have less reproductive success.<sup id="cite_ref-mating_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mating-231"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>229<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MacDonald_48-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Promiscuity#Other_animals" title="Promiscuity">Promiscuity</a>, where both sexes mate with multiple partners, exists in species like the Mexican free-tailed bat and the little brown bat.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>230<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>231<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There appears to be bias towards certain males among females in these bats.<sup id="cite_ref-MacDonald_48-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In a few species, such as the yellow-winged bat and spectral bat, adult males and females form <a href="/wiki/Monogamy_in_animals" title="Monogamy in animals">monogamous</a> pairs.<sup id="cite_ref-MacDonald_48-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015197_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015197-234"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>232<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lek_mating" title="Lek mating">Lek mating</a>, where males aggregate and compete for female choice through display, is rare in bats<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>233<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but occurs in the hammerheaded bat.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>234<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
