</p><p>For temperate living bats, mating takes place in late summer and early autumn.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAltringham2011105_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAltringham2011105-237"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>235<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Tropical bats may mate during the dry season.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>236<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> After copulation, the male may leave behind a <a href="/wiki/Mating_plug" title="Mating plug">mating plug</a> to block the sperm of other males and thus ensure his paternity.<sup id="cite_ref-CrichtonKrutzsch2000_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CrichtonKrutzsch2000-239"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>237<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In hibernating species, males are known to mate with females in torpor.<sup id="cite_ref-MacDonald_48-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Female bats use a variety of strategies to control the timing of pregnancy and the birth of young, to make delivery coincide with maximum food ability and other ecological factors. Females of some species have delayed fertilisation, in which sperm is stored in the reproductive tract for several months after mating. Mating occurs in late summer to early autumn but fertilisation does not occur until the following late winter to early spring. Other species exhibit <a href="/wiki/Delayed_implantation" class="mw-redirect" title="Delayed implantation">delayed implantation</a>, in which the egg is fertilised after mating, but remains free in the reproductive tract until external conditions become favourable for giving birth and caring for the offspring.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAltringham2011114–119_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAltringham2011114–119-240"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>238<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In another strategy, fertilisation and implantation both occur, but development of the foetus is delayed until good conditions prevail. During the delayed development the mother keeps the fertilised egg alive with nutrients. This process can go on for a long period, because of the advanced gas exchange system.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>239<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Pipistrellus_pipistrellus_baby.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Pipistrellus_pipistrellus_baby.jpg/250px-Pipistrellus_pipistrellus_baby.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Pipistrellus_pipistrellus_baby.jpg/330px-Pipistrellus_pipistrellus_baby.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Pipistrellus_pipistrellus_baby.jpg/500px-Pipistrellus_pipistrellus_baby.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a><figcaption>Newborn common pipistrelle, <i>Pipistrellus pipistrellus</i></figcaption></figure>
<p>For temperate living bats, births typically take place in May or June in the Northern Hemisphere; births in the Southern Hemisphere occur in November and December. Tropical species give birth at the beginning of the rainy season.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>240<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In most bat species, females carry and give birth to a single pup per litter.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>241<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> At birth, a bat pup can be up to 40 percent of the mother's weight,<sup id="cite_ref-MacDonald_48-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and the pelvic girdle of the female can expand during birth as the two-halves are connected by a flexible ligament.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFenton2001166_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFenton2001166-244"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>242<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Females typically give birth in a head-up or horizontal position, using gravity to make birthing easier. The young emerges rear-first, possibly to prevent the wings from getting tangled, and the female cradles it in her wing and tail membranes. In many species, females give birth and raise their young in <a href="/wiki/Maternity_colony_(bats)" class="mw-redirect" title="Maternity colony (bats)">maternity colonies</a> and may assist each other in birthing.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>243<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAltringham2011119_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAltringham2011119-246"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>244<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFenton2001166_244-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFenton2001166-244"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>242<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Most of the care for a young bat comes from the mother. In monogamous species, the father plays a role. Allo-suckling, where a female suckles another mother's young, occurs in several species. This may serve to increase colony size in species where females return to their natal colony to breed.<sup id="cite_ref-MacDonald_48-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A young bat's ability to fly coincides with the development of an adult body and forelimb length. For the little brown bat, this occurs about eighteen days after birth. Weaning of young for most species takes place in under eighty days. The common vampire bat nurses its offspring beyond that and young vampire bats achieve independence later in life than other species. This is probably due to the species' blood-based diet, which is difficult to obtain on a nightly basis.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015171_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFentonSimmons2015171-247"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>245<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Life_expectancy">Life expectancy</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Life expectancy"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Bat_Scientists_Lauri_Lutsar.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/The_Bat_Scientists_Lauri_Lutsar.jpg/220px-The_Bat_Scientists_Lauri_Lutsar.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/The_Bat_Scientists_Lauri_Lutsar.jpg/330px-The_Bat_Scientists_Lauri_Lutsar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/The_Bat_Scientists_Lauri_Lutsar.jpg/440px-The_Bat_Scientists_Lauri_Lutsar.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6720" data-file-height="4480" /></a><figcaption>The bat scientist Lauri Lutsar is checking the age of the bat he is holding as part of a national monitoring program in <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>The maximum lifespan of bats is three-and-a-half times longer than other mammals of similar size. Six species have been recorded to live over thirty years in the wild: the brown long-eared bat (<i>Plecotus auritus</i>), the little brown bat (<i>Myotis lucifugus</i>), the <a href="/wiki/Siberian_bat" title="Siberian bat">Siberian bat</a> (<i>Myotis sibiricus</i>), the <a href="/wiki/Lesser_mouse-eared_bat" title="Lesser mouse-eared bat">lesser mouse-eared bat</a> (<i>Myotis blythii</i>) the <a href="/wiki/Greater_horseshoe_bat" title="Greater horseshoe bat">greater horseshoe bat</a> (<i>Rhinolophus ferrumequinum</i>), and the <a href="/wiki/Indian_flying_fox" title="Indian flying fox">Indian flying fox</a> (<i>Pteropus giganteus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>246<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> One hypothesis consistent with the <a href="/wiki/Rate-of-living_theory" title="Rate-of-living theory">rate-of-living theory</a> links this to the fact that they slow down their <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_rate" class="mw-redirect" title="Metabolic rate">metabolic rate</a> while <a href="/wiki/Hibernating" class="mw-redirect" title="Hibernating">hibernating</a>; bats that hibernate, on average, have a longer lifespan than bats that do not.<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>247<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wilkinson2002_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wilkinson2002-250"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>248<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Another hypothesis is that flying has reduced their mortality rate, which would also be true for birds and gliding mammals. Bat species that give birth to multiple pups generally have a shorter lifespan than species that give birth to only a single pup. Cave-roosting species may have a longer lifespan than non-roosting species because of the decreased predation in caves. A male Siberian bat was recaptured in the wild after 41 years, making it the oldest known bat.<sup id="cite_ref-wilkinson2002_250-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wilkinson2002-250"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>248<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>249<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Interactions_with_humans">Interactions with humans</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Interactions with humans"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Human_uses_of_bats" title="Human uses of bats">Human uses of bats</a></div>

	<div class="pie5" style="background:#762a83;color:#000; transform: rotate(0.162turn)" title="Near-threatened (6.7%)"></div>
	<div class="pie5" style="background:#762a83;color:#000; transform: rotate(0.179turn)" title="Near-threatened (6.7%)"></div>
	<div class="pie50" style="background:#af8dc3;color:#000; transform: rotate(0.229turn)" title="Least concern (58%)"></div>
	<div class="pie50" style="background:#af8dc3;color:#000; transform: rotate(0.309turn)" title="Least concern (58%)"></div>
	<div class="pie12-5" style="background:#e7d4e8;color:#000; transform: rotate(0.809turn)" title="Data deficient (18.4%)"></div>
	<div class="pie12-5" style="background:#e7d4e8;color:#000; transform: rotate(0.868turn)" title="Data deficient (18.4%)"></div>
</div>
</div></div></div>
<p>Groups such as the <a href="/wiki/Bat_Conservation_International" title="Bat Conservation International">Bat Conservation International</a><sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>251<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> aim to increase awareness of bats' ecological roles and the environmental threats they face. This group called for Bat Appreciation Week from October 24–31 every year to promote awareness on the ecological importance of bats.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>252<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the United Kingdom, all bats are protected under the <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_and_Countryside_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Wildlife and Countryside Act">Wildlife and Countryside Acts</a>, and disturbing a bat or its roost can be punished with a heavy fine.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>253<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
In <a href="/wiki/Sarawak" title="Sarawak">Sarawak</a>, Malaysia, "all bats"<sup id="cite_ref-WPO1998_256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WPO1998-256"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>254<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> are protected under the <a href="/wiki/Malaysian_Wildlife_Law" title="Malaysian Wildlife Law">Wildlife Protection Ordinance 1998</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-WPO1998_256-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WPO1998-256"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>254<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but species such as the <a href="/wiki/Hairless_bat" title="Hairless bat">hairless bat</a> (<i>Cheiromeles torquatus</i>) are still eaten by the local communities.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>255<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Humans have caused the extinction of several species of bat in modern history, the most recent being the <a href="/wiki/Christmas_Island_pipistrelle" title="Christmas Island pipistrelle">Christmas Island pipistrelle</a> (<i>Pipistrellus murrayi</i>), which was declared extinct in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>256<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Many people put up bat houses to attract bats.<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>257<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The 1991 <a href="/wiki/University_of_Florida" title="University of Florida">University of Florida</a> bat house is the largest occupied artificial roost in the world, with around 400,000 residents.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>258<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In Britain, thickwalled and partly underground <a href="/wiki/British_hardened_field_defences_of_World_War_II" title="British hardened field defences of World War II">World War II pillboxes</a> have been converted to make roosts for bats,<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>259<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>260<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and purpose-built bat houses are occasionally built to mitigate damage to habitat from road or other developments.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>261<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>262<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cave_gate" title="Cave gate">Cave gates</a> are sometimes installed to limit human entry into caves with sensitive or endangered bat species. The gates are designed not to limit the airflow, and thus to maintain the cave's micro-ecosystem.<sup id="cite_ref-AGENCY_265-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AGENCY-265"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>263<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Of the 47 species of bats found in the United States, 35 are known to use human structures, including buildings and bridges. Fourteen species use bat houses.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>264<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Bat_as_food" title="Bat as food">Bats are eaten</a> in countries across Africa, Asia and the Pacific Rim. In some cases, such as in Guam, flying foxes have become endangered through being hunted for food.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>265<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There is evidence that suggests that <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine" title="Wind turbine">wind turbines</a> might create sufficient <a href="/wiki/Barotrauma" title="Barotrauma">barotrauma</a> (pressure damage) to kill bats.<sup id="cite_ref-BaerwaldD&#39;Amours2008_268-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BaerwaldD&#39;Amours2008-268"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>266<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Bats have typical <a href="/wiki/Lung#Mammalian_lungs" title="Lung">mammalian lungs</a>, which are thought to be more sensitive to sudden air pressure changes than <a href="/wiki/Avian_lungs" class="mw-redirect" title="Avian lungs">the lungs of birds</a>, making them more liable to fatal rupture.<sup id="cite_ref-nww_269-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nww-269"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>267<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>268<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>269<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>270<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>271<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Bats may be attracted to turbines, perhaps seeking roosts, increasing the death rate.<sup id="cite_ref-nww_269-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nww-269"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>267<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Acoustic deterrents may help to reduce bat mortality at wind farms.<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>272<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Diagnosis" title="Diagnosis">diagnosis</a> and contribution of barotrauma to bat deaths near wind turbine blades have been disputed by other research comparing dead bats found near wind turbines with bats killed by impact with buildings in areas with no turbines.<sup id="cite_ref-Rollins_et_al._275-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rollins_et_al.-275"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>273<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Cultural_significance">Cultural significance</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Cultural significance"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Francisco_Jos%C3%A9_de_Goya_y_Lucientes_-_The_sleep_of_reason_produces_monsters_(No._43),_from_Los_Caprichos_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Francisco_Jos%C3%A9_de_Goya_y_Lucientes_-_The_sleep_of_reason_produces_monsters_%28No._43%29%2C_from_Los_Caprichos_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/250px-Francisco_Jos%C3%A9_de_Goya_y_Lucientes_-_The_sleep_of_reason_produces_monsters_%28No._43%29%2C_from_Los_Caprichos_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="257" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Francisco_Jos%C3%A9_de_Goya_y_Lucientes_-_The_sleep_of_reason_produces_monsters_%28No._43%29%2C_from_Los_Caprichos_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Francisco_Jos%C3%A9_de_Goya_y_Lucientes_-_The_sleep_of_reason_produces_monsters_%28No._43%29%2C_from_Los_Caprichos_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Francisco_Jos%C3%A9_de_Goya_y_Lucientes_-_The_sleep_of_reason_produces_monsters_%28No._43%29%2C_from_Los_Caprichos_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/500px-Francisco_Jos%C3%A9_de_Goya_y_Lucientes_-_The_sleep_of_reason_produces_monsters_%28No._43%29%2C_from_Los_Caprichos_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2645" data-file-height="4000" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Francisco_de_Goya" class="mw-redirect" title="Francisco de Goya">Francisco de Goya</a>, <i>The Sleep of Reason Produces Monsters</i>, 1797</figcaption></figure>
<p>Since bats are mammals, yet can fly, they are considered to be <a href="/wiki/Liminal_being" title="Liminal being">liminal beings</a> in various traditions.<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>274<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In many cultures, including in Europe, bats are associated with darkness, death, witchcraft, and malevolence.<sup id="cite_ref-symbols_277-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-symbols-277"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>275<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Among <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native Americans</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Creek_(people)" class="mw-redirect" title="Creek (people)">Creek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cherokee" title="Cherokee">Cherokee</a> and <a href="/wiki/Apache_Tribe" class="mw-redirect" title="Apache Tribe">Apache</a>, the bat is identified as a <a href="/wiki/Trickster" title="Trickster">trickster</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>276<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In Tanzania, a winged batlike creature known as <a href="/wiki/Popobawa" title="Popobawa">Popobawa</a> is believed to be a shapeshifting <a href="/wiki/Evil_spirit" class="mw-redirect" title="Evil spirit">evil spirit</a> that assaults and <a href="/wiki/Sodomises" class="mw-redirect" title="Sodomises">sodomises</a> its victims.<sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>277<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Aztec" class="mw-redirect" title="Aztec">Aztec</a> <a href="/wiki/Mythology" class="mw-redirect" title="Mythology">mythology</a>, bats symbolised the land of the dead, destruction, and decay.<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>278<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReadGonzalez_281-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReadGonzalez-281"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>279<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>280<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> An East Nigerian tale tells that the bat developed its nocturnal habits after causing the death of his partner, the bush-rat, and now hides by day to avoid arrest.<sup id="cite_ref-Arnott1962_283-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arnott1962-283"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>281<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>More positive depictions of bats exist in some cultures. In China, bats have been associated with happiness, joy and good fortune. Five bats are used to symbolise the "Five Blessings": longevity, wealth, health, love of virtue and peaceful death.<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>282<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The bat is sacred in <a href="/wiki/Tonga" title="Tonga">Tonga</a> and is often considered the physical manifestation of a separable <a href="/wiki/Soul_(spirit)" class="mw-redirect" title="Soul (spirit)">soul</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>283<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Zapotec_civilisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Zapotec civilisation">Zapotec civilisation</a> of Mesoamerica, the bat god presided over corn and fertility.<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>284<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Bat_god,_Zapotec,_Period_III-A_-_Mesoamerican_objects_in_the_American_Museum_of_Natural_History_-_DSC06023.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Bat_god%2C_Zapotec%2C_Period_III-A_-_Mesoamerican_objects_in_the_American_Museum_of_Natural_History_-_DSC06023.JPG/250px-Bat_god%2C_Zapotec%2C_Period_III-A_-_Mesoamerican_objects_in_the_American_Museum_of_Natural_History_-_DSC06023.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Bat_god%2C_Zapotec%2C_Period_III-A_-_Mesoamerican_objects_in_the_American_Museum_of_Natural_History_-_DSC06023.JPG/330px-Bat_god%2C_Zapotec%2C_Period_III-A_-_Mesoamerican_objects_in_the_American_Museum_of_Natural_History_-_DSC06023.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Bat_god%2C_Zapotec%2C_Period_III-A_-_Mesoamerican_objects_in_the_American_Museum_of_Natural_History_-_DSC06023.JPG/500px-Bat_god%2C_Zapotec%2C_Period_III-A_-_Mesoamerican_objects_in_the_American_Museum_of_Natural_History_-_DSC06023.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3240" data-file-height="4320" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Zapotec_civilization" title="Zapotec civilization">Zapotec</a> bat god, <a href="/wiki/Oaxaca" title="Oaxaca">Oaxaca</a>, 350–500 CE</figcaption></figure>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Weird_Sisters" class="mw-redirect" title="Weird Sisters">Weird Sisters</a> in Shakespeare's <i><a href="/wiki/Macbeth" title="Macbeth">Macbeth</a></i> used the fur of a bat in their brew.<sup id="cite_ref-deVries76_287-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deVries76-287"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>285<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western culture</a>, the bat is often a symbol of the night and its foreboding nature. The bat is a primary animal associated with fictional characters of the night, both villainous <a href="/wiki/Vampire" title="Vampire">vampires</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Count_Dracula" title="Count Dracula">Count Dracula</a> and before him <i><a href="/wiki/Varney_the_Vampire" title="Varney the Vampire">Varney the Vampire</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>286<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and heroes, such as the <a href="/wiki/DC_Comics" title="DC Comics">DC Comics</a> character <a href="/wiki/Batman" title="Batman">Batman</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Fleisher_289-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fleisher-289"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>287<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Oppel" title="Kenneth Oppel">Kenneth Oppel</a>'s Silverwing novels narrate the adventures of a young bat,<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>288<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> based on the <a href="/wiki/Silver-haired_bat" title="Silver-haired bat">silver-haired bat</a> of North America.<sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>289<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The bat is sometimes used as a <a href="/wiki/Bat_(heraldry)" title="Bat (heraldry)">heraldic symbol</a> in Spain and France, appearing in the coats of arms of the towns of <a href="/wiki/Valencia" title="Valencia">Valencia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palma_de_Mallorca" title="Palma de Mallorca">Palma de Mallorca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fraga" title="Fraga">Fraga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Albacete" title="Albacete">Albacete</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Montchauvet,_Yvelines" title="Montchauvet, Yvelines">Montchauvet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cervantesvirtual.com_292-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cervantesvirtual.com-292"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>290<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-293" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-293"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>291<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ifc.dpz.es_294-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ifc.dpz.es-294"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>292<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Three US states have an official <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._state_bats" class="mw-redirect" title="List of U.S. state bats">state bat</a>. Texas and Oklahoma are represented by the Mexican free-tailed bat, while Virginia is represented by the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_big-eared_bat" title="Virginia big-eared bat">Virginia big-eared bat</a> (<i>Corynorhinus townsendii virginianus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>293<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Economics">Economics</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Economics"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Insectivorous bats in particular are especially helpful to farmers, as they control populations of agricultural pests and reduce the need to use <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a>. It has been estimated that bats save the agricultural industry of the United States anywhere from <a href="/wiki/USD" class="mw-redirect" title="USD">$</a>3.7<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>billion to $53<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>billion per year in pesticides and damage to crops. This also prevents the overuse of pesticides, which can pollute the surrounding environment, and may lead to resistance in future generations of insects.<sup id="cite_ref-296" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-296"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>294<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Bat dung, a type of <a href="/wiki/Guano" title="Guano">guano</a>, is rich in nitrates and is mined from caves for use as <a href="/wiki/Fertiliser" class="mw-redirect" title="Fertiliser">fertiliser</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-297" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-297"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>295<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/US_Civil_War" class="mw-redirect" title="US Civil War">US Civil War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saltpetre" class="mw-redirect" title="Saltpetre">saltpetre</a> was collected from caves to make <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">gunpowder</a>.  At the time, it was believed that the nitrate all came from the bat guano, but it is now known that most of it is produced by <a href="/wiki/Nitrifying_bacteria" title="Nitrifying bacteria">nitrifying bacteria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>296<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ann_W._Richards_Congress_Avenue_Bridge" title="Ann W. Richards Congress Avenue Bridge">Congress Avenue Bridge</a> in <a href="/wiki/Austin,_Texas" title="Austin, Texas">Austin, Texas</a>, is the summer home to North America's largest urban bat colony, an estimated 1,500,000 Mexican free-tailed bats. About 100,000 tourists a year visit the bridge at twilight to watch the bats leave the roost.<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>297<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-291"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-291">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite id="CITEREFOppel" class="citation web cs1">Oppel, K. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20170926043235/http://www.kennethoppel.ca/silverwing/shade.shtml">"The Characters: Shade"</a>. Their bodies are about the same size as mice. They're insectivores, which means they eat only insects." – K.O.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=The+Characters%3A+Shade&amp;rft.pub=Kenneth+Oppel&amp;rft.aulast=Oppel&amp;rft.aufirst=K.&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.kennethoppel.ca%2Fsilverwing%2Fshade.shtml&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABat" class="Z3988"></span></span>
